The Mu'tazila's Qur'anic Proof Against the Vision of Allah
Author reports the Mu'tazila's claim and their Qur'anic proof against vision of Allah, along with their argument that the negation of perception is a self-praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' proves the impossibility of seeing Allah in both this world and the Hereafter, and why they claim it would negate Allah's self-praise if vision were possible.

Core Idea
The negation of vision is a self-praise that would be meaningless if vision were possible.

The opponents of the vision of Allah, including the Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Zaydiyya, argue that Allah's statement 'Eyesight does not perceive Him' (6:103) is a self-praise that negates vision absolutely. They reason that if vision were possible, even only in the Hereafter, this negation would not be praise because it would only deny a permissible thing. This lesson unpacks their proof and the underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponents' claim
Read the passage where the Mu'tazila etc. assert that vision of Allah is impossible in both worlds because impossibility does not change with time or place.
They base this on the assumption that vision would imply something impossible for Allah.
2
Analyze the Qur'anic proof
Focus on the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' and how the opponents argue it is a self-praise that negates vision absolutely, similar to other self-praises like 'بديع السموات والأرض'.
They use the context of the verse to claim it establishes perfection by negating what is unbefitting.
3
Reflect on the logical implication
Consider why the opponents argue that if vision were possible in the Hereafter, the negation would not be praise. This is because negation of a permissible thing is not a source of praise. Therefore, the verse must indicate impossibility in both worlds.
This is the core of their argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة والنجارية والزيدية من الروافض : إن الله تعالى ليس بجائز الرؤية، بل يستحيل ذلك عليه ولا يراه أحد لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة، لأن المحال لا يستبدل باختلاف الأزمنة والدهور، ولا باختلاف الأمكنة والبقاع فلن يصير جائزا البتة، فلا يرى لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة وإنما اختلفوا لأن الخصوم ظنوا أن القول بالرؤية يوجب إثبات ما يستحيل على الله تعالى وأهل الحق قد عرفوا أنه لا يؤدي إلى ذلك.
[أدلة الخصوم على نفي الرؤية]:
احتج الخصوم في ذلك بقوله تعالى: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ الآية فالله تعالى تمدح بانتفاء الرؤية عن ذاته، إذ الإدراك بالبصر ليس إلا الرؤية كما تمدح بأسمائه الحسنى وصفاته العلا في سياق الآية وسياقها، فكان حاصل الآية التمدح بثبوت هذه الصفات اللاتي هي صفات الكمال، وبنفي ما لا يليق بصفاته، وفي إثبات ما نفى إزالة التمدح وإثبات النقص كما هو في نفي ما أثبت بقوله: بَدِيعُ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وقوله: خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَكِيلٌ وقوله: وَهُوَ اللَّطِيفُ الْخَبِيرُ وذلك لا يجوز وليس يستقيم التمدح بنفي الإدراك في الدنيا دون الآخرة، لأن إثبات ما تمدح بنفيه نقص، وإثبات النقص عليه وإزالة ما به يتمدح لا يجوز لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة، وهذا لأن إثبات النقص إثبات دلالة الحدوث، والله تعالى منزه عن سمات الحدوث، وجميع أنواع العيوب والنقص في الدنيا والآخرة، إذ لو جاز ذلك في حال لانقلب محدثا وذلك محال. ولأن الله تعالى عندكم جائز الرؤية في الدنيا، فلم يكن التمدح بنفي ما يجوز عليه متحققا ألا ترى أنه تعالى لا يتمدح أنه لم يخلق لزيد ولدا وذلك جائز فلم يكن نفيه مدحا، وهذا لأن ما كان في حد الجواز لم يكن ثبوته موجبا للنقص فلم يكن نفيه مدحا، وإذا تمدح بنفي الإدراك، دل أنه لا يجوز لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة.
And the Mu'tazila, the Najjariyya, and the Zaydiyya from the Rawafid said: Indeed Allah Most High is not permissible of vision, rather it is impossible for Him, and no one sees Him, neither in this world nor in the Hereafter, because the impossible does not change with difference of times and ages, nor with difference of places and regions, so it will never become permissible at all. So He is not seen neither in this world nor in the Hereafter. And they only differed because the opponents thought that the statement of vision necessitates affirming what is impossible for Allah Most High, while the people of truth have known that it does not lead to that.
[Proofs of the opponents for negating vision]:
The opponents argued in that with His statement Most High: "Eyesight does not perceive Him" (6:103) so Allah Most High praised Himself by negating vision from His essence, for perception by sight is nothing but vision, just as He praised Himself with His beautiful names and high attributes in the context of the verse and its sequence. So the outcome of the verse is praise by affirming these attributes which are attributes of perfection, and by negating what is not befitting His attributes. And in affirming what He negated is removal of praise and affirmation of deficiency, just as it is in negating what He affirmed, as in His statement: "Originator of the heavens and the earth" (6:101), and His statement: "Creator of everything, and He is over everything a trustee" (39:62), and His statement: "And He is the Subtle, the Aware" (6:103). And that is not permissible, and it is not correct to praise by negating perception in this world without the Hereafter, because affirming what He praised by negating is a deficiency, and affirming deficiency upon Him and removing that by which He is praised is not permissible neither in this world nor in the Hereafter. This is because affirming deficiency is affirming an indication of temporality (huduth), and Allah Most High is transcendent above characteristics of temporality (samat al-huduth) and all kinds of defects and deficiency in this world and the Hereafter. For if that were permissible in any state, He would turn into something originated (muhdath), and that is impossible. And because Allah Most High according to you is permissible of vision in this world, so the praise by negating what is permissible upon Him would not be realized. Do you not see that He Most High does not praise Himself that He did not create for Zayd a son, and that is permissible? So its negation is not praise. This is because what is in the category of permissibility, its existence does not necessitate deficiency, so its negation is not praise. And when He praised Himself by negating perception, it indicates that it is not permissible neither in this world nor in the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage reports the position of the Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Zaydi Rawafid that vision of Allah is impossible in both this world and the Hereafter. They argue that what is impossible does not become possible over time or place. The author notes that the opponents mistakenly think affirming vision would lead to affirming impossibility for Allah, while the truth is otherwise. Then the author presents the opponents' first proof: the Quranic verse "Eyesight does not perceive Him" (6:103). They argue that Allah praises Himself by negating vision, just as He praises Himself with His beautiful names and high attributes in the same context. Thus, the verse affirms perfection attributes and negates what is unbefitting. If one were to affirm what Allah negated, it would remove the praise and affirm deficiency, which is analogous to negating what He affirmed in other verses like "Originator of the heavens and the earth" etc. The argument then explains that such a deficiency would imply temporality (huduth), which is impossible for Allah. The author then refutes the possibility that negation of vision applies only to this world by arguing that if vision were possible in the Hereafter, Allah's self-praise for negating it in this world would be meaningless, because negation of a permissible thing is not praise, just as one does not praise himself for not having a son (since that is permissible). Therefore, the verse indicates that vision is impossible in both worlds.

إدراك
تمددح
حدوث
جواز
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' only denies vision in this world, allowing it in the Hereafter.
Correction: The passage argues that if vision were possible in the Hereafter, the verse would not be a genuine self-praise, because negation of a permissible thing is not praise. Thus the negation must be absolute.

Checkpoint
Why do the opponents argue that if vision of Allah were possible in the Hereafter, the verse 'Eyesight does not perceive Him' would not be praise?
A) Because vision would still be impossible in this world.
B) Because the negation of a permissible thing is not a source of praise.
C) Because the verse only refers to the Hereafter.
D) Because Allah cannot be seen anyway.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' to argue that vision of Allah is impossible in both worlds, because if it were possible, Allah's self-praise would be meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah praises Himself for not being seen, does it humble you that your Creator is beyond your perception, or does it make you feel distant? What does this teach you about approaching Him with awe and reverence?
?
If you were to insist that Allah must be seen to be truly known, would you be demanding that He submit to your expectations? How can you cultivate trust in His self-description even when it challenges your reasoning?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that affirming vision would imply deficiency in Allah. Is there an area of your life where you are afraid that submitting to Allah's revelation might imply a 'deficiency' in your own understanding? Are you willing to let go of that fear?

The Mu'tazila Argument Against Vision of Allah
Mu'tazila rational argument from analogy against vision of Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that vision requires a body, and why they concluded that Allah—being not a body—cannot be seen.

Core Idea
Visibility requires bodiliness, so Allah—being not a body—cannot be seen.

The Mu'tazila and others who deny the vision of Allah relied on a rational argument based on analogy from the visible world. In this passage, the author presents their reasoning: vision in the witnessed world has a cause and conditions that are specific to bodies, and since Allah is not a body, the ruling of visibility cannot be transferred to Him. Let's examine their argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understanding the Principle of Analogy
Learn that inference from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib) requires that the cause (illa) of the ruling be transferable. If the cause is specific to the witnessed, the ruling cannot extend.
This principle is key to the Mu'tazila's argument.
2
Identifying the Cause of Visibility
The passage states that the cause of a thing being visible in the witness is its being a body (jism). Every body is seen, but what lies beyond bodies (accidents) cannot be seen.
Visibility is tied to bodiliness.
3
Applying the Principle to Allah
Since Allah is not a body, the cause of visibility does not apply to Him. Therefore, the ruling of visibility cannot be transferred from witness to unseen.
This is the core conclusion.
4
Noting Additional Views
The passage mentions al-Nazzam and his followers, who only allow vision of bodies, and some philosophers who restrict vision to colors. These support the main argument.
These are supplementary positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة العقلية للنافين للرؤية:
والمعقول لهم أن للرؤية في الشاهد علة وشروطا، لن يتصور تعدية ذلك إلى الغائب، وما يثبت في الغائب أو ينفى عنه يكون بطريق الاستدلال من الشاهد على الغائب وما تعدت العلة فيه، تعدى الحكم وما لا، فلا، وقد مر هذا فيما تقدم، فإذا لم يمكن تعدية علة الرؤية وشروطها لن يمكن تعدية الرؤية. وإنما قلنا ذلك لأن علة كون الشيء في الشاهد مرئيا كونه جسما، إذ كل جسم يرى ويستحيل رؤية ما وراء الجسم من الموجودات وهي الأعراض، ولا تعدي للجسم فلن يتعدى الحكم، وهو كونه صالحا لأن يرى.
هذا هو طريقة النظام ومن تابعه من المعتزلة، فإنهم لم يجوزوا في الشاهد إلا رؤية الأجسام وقالوا بإحالة رؤية ما وراء الأجسام، وتعلقوا بالوجود في الشاهد كذا وجد، وبعض الفلاسفة لم يجوز تعلق الرؤية إلا بالألوان.
Rational proofs of those who deny vision:
And what is rationally understandable for them is that vision in the witness (al-shahid) has a cause (illa) and conditions (shurut), and it is inconceivable to extend that to the unseen (al-gha'ib). What is affirmed of the unseen or negated of it is by way of inference (istidlal) from the witness to the unseen, and whatever the cause has extended in it, the ruling extends, and whatever not, then not. And this has passed earlier. So if the cause and conditions of vision cannot be extended, then vision cannot be extended. And we only said that because the cause of a thing being visible in the witness is its being a body (jism), for every body is seen, and it is impossible to see what is beyond the body from existing things, which are the accidents (a'rad). And the body cannot be extended, so the ruling will not be extended, which is its being suitable to be seen.
This is the method of al-Nazzam and those who followed him from the Mu'tazila, for they did not permit in the witness except vision of bodies, and they said it is impossible to see what is beyond bodies, and they clung to the existence in the witness as such. And some philosophers did not permit attachment of vision except to colors.
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage presents the rational proof of the Mu'tazila and others who deny the vision of Allah. It begins by stating the logical principle: the cause (illa) and conditions of vision in the witnessed world (al-shahid) cannot be transferred to the unseen (al-gha'ib). The method of inference (istidlal) from the witness to the unseen requires that the cause be transferable; if it is not, the ruling (hukm) does not transfer. The passage then identifies the cause of visibility in the witness: a thing is visible because it is a body (jism). Since every body is seen, but what lies beyond bodies—namely accidents (a'rad)—cannot be seen, and because the body itself cannot be extended to Allah (who is not a body), the ruling of visibility cannot be extended. The passage then mentions al-Nazzam and his followers among the Mu'tazila, who held that only bodies are seen in the witness, and therefore it is impossible to see what is beyond bodies (i.e., non-bodily entities). They rely on empirical observation. Some philosophers further restrict vision only to colors. This argument aims to show that since Allah is not a body, He cannot be seen.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
علة
شروط
جسم
عرض
استدلال
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any analogy from the visible world can apply to Allah.
Correction: The passage shows that the cause (illa) must be transferable; since visibility's cause is bodiliness and Allah is not a body, the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, what is the cause (illa) of a thing being visible in the witness?
A) Its being an accident.
B) Its being a body.
C) Its having color.
D) Its being in the world.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that visibility requires bodiliness, so Allah—being not a body—cannot be seen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a rational argument that Allah cannot be seen because He is not a body. But deep down, do you feel a longing to see your Lord? Is your heart satisfied with intellectual reasoning, or does it yearn for the vision promised to the believers? Today, will you humbly admit that your intellect has limits and that you trust Allah's promise over your own reasoning?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that what is true in the witnessed world must apply to Allah. Have you ever unknowingly imposed your own limited experiences on Allah? For example, expecting Him to act like a human or to fit your sense of justice? Will you repent from such unspoken assumptions and submit to what Allah has revealed about Himself, even if it defies your logic?
?
This argument denies vision of Allah based on the absence of bodily qualities. But what about your own relationship with the unseen? Do you live as if Allah's attributes are distant and abstract, or do you cultivate a heart that feels His presence? Will you today turn from dry arguments to a living faith that yearns for the beatific vision, despite not understanding how it will be?

Views on Visible Objects in the Witnessed World
Enumeration of views on visible objects by al-Nazzam, philosophers, al-Najjariyya, al-Jubba'i, and Abu Hashim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions of Mu'tazili and early theologians on what objects are visible in the witnessed world, and why none of these positions imply that God can be seen.

Core Idea
Different groups agreed on the impossibility of seeing God, but disagreed on what can be seen in the world.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a detailed overview of several theological schools and their views on which objects are perceptible by sight. Al-Nazzam, al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, and others each had distinct criteria for visibility. Understanding their arguments helps clarify why they all concluded that God—being beyond bodies, accidents, and spatial constraints—cannot be seen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the different groups
Carefully read the Arabic and translation to identify the views of al-Nazzam, the philosophers, al-Najjariyya, al-Jubba'i, and Abu Hashim. Note what each considers visible.
Pay attention to how each group restricts visibility differently.
2
Compare the restrictiveness of each view
Order the views from most restrictive (only bodies) to least (bodies, colors, and spatial determinations). Consider which view allows the most objects to be seen.
Al-Jubba'i allows the most; Abu Hashim excludes spatial determinations.
3
Notice the common conclusion about the unseen
All groups agree that none of these visible categories transfer to the unseen (God). Reflect on why this is significant for the debate on seeing God.
The passage explicitly states: 'nothing of that transfers to the unseen'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا هو طريقة النظام ومن تابعه من المعتزلة، فإنهم لم يجوزوا في الشاهد إلا رؤية الأجسام وقالوا بإحالة رؤية ما وراء الأجسام، وتعلقوا بالوجود في الشاهد كذا وجد، وبعض الفلاسفة لم يجوز تعلق الرؤية إلا بالألوان. وكذا المحكي عن النجارية هذا، وأنكر هؤلاء رؤية الأجسام ورؤية ما وراء الألوان من الأعراض. وتعلق هؤلاء أيضا بمجرد الوجود. وزعم الجبائي أن بعض الأعراض وهو الألوان وأنواعها معروفة، والأكوان الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق والقرب والبعد يرى، كما أن الأجسام ترى، فكانت الرؤية عنده متعلقة بأحد هذه المعاني، وهي الجسم واللون والكون وزعم أبو هاشم أن المرئي هي الأجسام والألوان، وأما الأكوان فليست بمرئية، وشيء من ذلك لا يتعدى إلى الغائب فلا يتعدى الحكم وهو جواز الرؤية، قالوا والشرط أيضا لا يتعدى فإن شرط الرؤية أن يكون بين الرائي والمرئي مسافة حتى إن المرئي لو التصق بآلة الرؤية انعدمت الرؤية، وكذا اتصال الشعاع وانطباع المرئي في آلة الرؤية شرط عند من لا يثبت الرؤية إلا الأجسام، فإن المذهب عند رئيسهم وهو النظام أن المحسوسات لا تدرك إلا بالمماسة المجاورة، فإن اليد لا تستدرك الحرارة والبرودة والملامسة والخشونة والصلابة واللين، إلا بالمحاسة الحاصلة بين آلة الحس وبين المحسوس، وكذا الطعوم لن تستدرك إلا بالمماسة الحاصلة بين آلة الذوق وهي اللسان واللهوات وبين الطعام، وكذا الشم على هذا فإن الرائحة عنده جسم ينفصل عن الجسم المشموم ويتصل بخياشيم الشام ليحصل له العلم بذلك، وكذا الصوت عنده جسم وهو الهواء المضغوط فينفصل عن الجسم فينتقل في الجو إلى أن يدخل صماخ السامع فيقع بينهما مماسة فيحصل له العلم بالصوت، فكذا لابد من مماسة حاصلة بين آلة الرؤية وبين المرئي فينفصل شعاع من عين الرائي فيتصل بالمرئي فيحصل بينهما مماسة فيعلم بالمرئي.
This is the method of al-Nazzam and those who followed him from among the Mu'tazila, for they did not permit in the witnessed world except the vision of bodies, and they said that vision of what is beyond bodies is impossible. And they relied on existence in the witnessed world as it is found. And some of the philosophers did not permit the attachment of vision except to colors. And likewise what is narrated from al-Najjariyya, and these denied the vision of bodies and vision of what is beyond colors from among the accidents. And these also relied on mere existence. And al-Jubba'i claimed that some accidents – and they are colors and their types are known, and the spatial determinations: motion, rest, conjunction, separation, proximity, and distance – are seen, just as bodies are seen. So vision with him is attached to one of these meanings: body, color, and spatial determination. And Abu Hashim claimed that the visible things are bodies and colors, but as for spatial determinations, they are not visible. And nothing of that transfers to the unseen, so the judgment does not transfer, which is the permissibility of vision. They said: and the condition also does not transfer. For the condition of vision is that there be a distance between the seer and the seen, such that if the seen were to stick to the organ of vision, vision would cease. And likewise the connection of the ray and the impression of the seen in the organ of vision is a condition according to those who do not affirm vision except for bodies. For the doctrine according to their leader, al-Nazzam, is that sensible things are perceived only by contiguous contact. For the hand does not perceive heat, cold, touch, roughness, hardness, and softness except by the sensation occurring between the sense organ and the sensible. Likewise tastes are not perceived except by the contact occurring between the organ of taste – which is the tongue and the palate – and the food. Likewise smell is upon this: for smell according to him is a body that separates from the smelled body and connects with the nostrils of the smeller so that knowledge of it is obtained. Likewise sound according to him is a body – compressed air – that separates from the body and travels through the air until it enters the ear canal of the hearer, then contact occurs between them and knowledge of the sound is obtained. So likewise, there must be a contact occurring between the organ of vision and the seen, so a ray separates from the eye of the seer and connects with the seen, then contact occurs between them and knowledge of the seen is obtained.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the views of various theologians on what objects are visible in the witnessed world. It begins with al-Nazzam's view that only bodies are visible; everything beyond bodies (accidents) is not visible. Some philosophers restrict vision to colors. Al-Najjariyya even deny the visibility of bodies and only affirm colors. Al-Jubba'i expands visibility to include certain accidents: colors and spatial determinations (akwan: motion, rest, etc.). Abu Hashim excludes spatial determinations, allowing only bodies and colors. The author then notes that despite these differences, all agree that none of these visible objects (bodies, colors, etc.) transfer to the unseen, so the judgment of visibility does not apply to God. The passage then explains the conditions of vision: distance between seer and seen, and according to al-Nazzam, the necessity of contact (mumassa) between the sense organ and the object. Al-Nazzam's theory of perception is described in detail: all senses require physical contact, with the sense organ receiving particles from the object (e.g., light rays from the eye). Since these conditions cannot be met in the case of God (who is not a body, not at a distance, etc.), vision of God is impossible.

Key Terms
النظام
الجبائي
أبو هاشم
النجارية
الأجسام
الألوان
الأكوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because al-Jubba'i allows vision of accidents, he might allow vision of God, but the passage explicitly states that none of this transfers to the unseen.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i and all groups hold that the visible categories in the witnessed world do not apply to God, so vision of God remains impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which theologian claimed that spatial determinations (akwan) are visible?
A) Al-Nazzam
B) Al-Jubba'i
C) Abu Hashim
D) The philosophers

Key Takeaway
The diversity of opinions on visibility in the witnessed world does not affect the conclusion that God cannot be seen, as none of the visible categories apply to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that even the most expansive view of visibility (al-Jubba'i's) does not apply to God, what assumption about God's nature do you need to actively reject in your heart? How does this shape your worship and awe of Him?
?
The philosophers and al-Najjariyya restrict visibility to colors. Yet you know colors are created. How does this reinforce that nothing in creation resembles God? What comfort or challenge does this give you when trying to know Him?
?
Why do you think the scholars debated so precisely what is visible? How does their precision expose the laziness of your own mind when thinking about God? What concrete step will you take today to guard your thoughts from imagining God like created things?

The Condition of Distance for Vision
Statement of the condition of distance for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why distance between seer and seen is a necessary condition for vision, and how this condition rules out vision of God.

Core Idea
Vision requires distance—God is not at a distance, so He cannot be seen.

The passage now turns to the conditions that must be met for vision to occur. The first condition is that there must be a distance between the seer and the seen object; if the object touches the eye, vision ceases. Since God is not located at a distance from us, this condition cannot be fulfilled, making vision of God impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition: distance is required
Read the passage stating that vision requires a distance between seer and seen, such that if the object touches the eye, vision is lost.
This is a condition that applies in the witnessed world.
2
Reflect on why this condition cannot apply to God
Consider that God is not a body and does not occupy space. Therefore, there is no distance between us and God that can be measured or fulfilled.
The condition of distance is physical, but God is beyond physicality.
3
See how this supports the impossibility of vision
Since the condition of distance is necessary for vision and cannot be met for God, vision of God is impossible. This is a key argument used by the Mu'tazila.
The passage also says the condition does not transfer to the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا هو طريقة النظام ومن تابعه من المعتزلة، فإنهم لم يجوزوا في الشاهد إلا رؤية الأجسام وقالوا بإحالة رؤية ما وراء الأجسام، وتعلقوا بالوجود في الشاهد كذا وجد، وبعض الفلاسفة لم يجوز تعلق الرؤية إلا بالألوان. وكذا المحكي عن النجارية هذا، وأنكر هؤلاء رؤية الأجسام ورؤية ما وراء الألوان من الأعراض. وتعلق هؤلاء أيضا بمجرد الوجود. وزعم الجبائي أن بعض الأعراض وهو الألوان وأنواعها معروفة، والأكوان الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق والقرب والبعد يرى، كما أن الأجسام ترى، فكانت الرؤية عنده متعلقة بأحد هذه المعاني، وهي الجسم واللون والكون وزعم أبو هاشم أن المرئي هي الأجسام والألوان، وأما الأكوان فليست بمرئية، وشيء من ذلك لا يتعدى إلى الغائب فلا يتعدى الحكم وهو جواز الرؤية، قالوا والشرط أيضا لا يتعدى فإن شرط الرؤية أن يكون بين الرائي والمرئي مسافة حتى إن المرئي لو التصق بآلة الرؤية انعدمت الرؤية، وكذا اتصال الشعاع وانطباع المرئي في آلة الرؤية شرط عند من لا يثبت الرؤية إلا الأجسام، فإن المذهب عند رئيسهم وهو النظام أن المحسوسات لا تدرك إلا بالمماسة المجاورة، فإن اليد لا تستدرك الحرارة والبرودة والملامسة والخشونة والصلابة واللين، إلا بالمحاسة الحاصلة بين آلة الحس وبين المحسوس، وكذا الطعوم لن تستدرك إلا بالمماسة الحاصلة بين آلة الذوق وهي اللسان واللهوات وبين الطعام، وكذا الشم على هذا فإن الرائحة عنده جسم ينفصل عن الجسم المشموم ويتصل بخياشيم الشام ليحصل له العلم بذلك، وكذا الصوت عنده جسم وهو الهواء المضغوط فينفصل عن الجسم فينتقل في الجو إلى أن يدخل صماخ السامع فيقع بينهما مماسة فيحصل له العلم بالصوت، فكذا لابد من مماسة حاصلة بين آلة الرؤية وبين المرئي فينفصل شعاع من عين الرائي فيتصل بالمرئي فيحصل بينهما مماسة فيعلم بالمرئي.
This is the method of al-Nazzam and those who followed him from among the Mu'tazila, for they did not permit in the witnessed world except the vision of bodies, and they said that vision of what is beyond bodies is impossible. And they relied on existence in the witnessed world as it is found. And some of the philosophers did not permit the attachment of vision except to colors. And likewise what is narrated from al-Najjariyya, and these denied the vision of bodies and vision of what is beyond colors from among the accidents. And these also relied on mere existence. And al-Jubba'i claimed that some accidents – and they are colors and their types are known, and the spatial determinations: motion, rest, conjunction, separation, proximity, and distance – are seen, just as bodies are seen. So vision with him is attached to one of these meanings: body, color, and spatial determination. And Abu Hashim claimed that the visible things are bodies and colors, but as for spatial determinations, they are not visible. And nothing of that transfers to the unseen, so the judgment does not transfer, which is the permissibility of vision. They said: and the condition also does not transfer. For the condition of vision is that there be a distance between the seer and the seen, such that if the seen were to stick to the organ of vision, vision would cease. And likewise the connection of the ray and the impression of the seen in the organ of vision is a condition according to those who do not affirm vision except for bodies. For the doctrine according to their leader, al-Nazzam, is that sensible things are perceived only by contiguous contact. For the hand does not perceive heat, cold, touch, roughness, hardness, and softness except by the sensation occurring between the sense organ and the sensible. Likewise tastes are not perceived except by the contact occurring between the organ of taste – which is the tongue and the palate – and the food. Likewise smell is upon this: for smell according to him is a body that separates from the smelled body and connects with the nostrils of the smeller so that knowledge of it is obtained. Likewise sound according to him is a body – compressed air – that separates from the body and travels through the air until it enters the ear canal of the hearer, then contact occurs between them and knowledge of the sound is obtained. So likewise, there must be a contact occurring between the organ of vision and the seen, so a ray separates from the eye of the seer and connects with the seen, then contact occurs between them and knowledge of the seen is obtained.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the views of various theologians on what objects are visible in the witnessed world. It begins with al-Nazzam's view that only bodies are visible; everything beyond bodies (accidents) is not visible. Some philosophers restrict vision to colors. Al-Najjariyya even deny the visibility of bodies and only affirm colors. Al-Jubba'i expands visibility to include certain accidents: colors and spatial determinations (akwan: motion, rest, etc.). Abu Hashim excludes spatial determinations, allowing only bodies and colors. The author then notes that despite these differences, all agree that none of these visible objects (bodies, colors, etc.) transfer to the unseen, so the judgment of visibility does not apply to God. The passage then explains the conditions of vision: distance between seer and seen, and according to al-Nazzam, the necessity of contact (mumassa) between the sense organ and the object. Al-Nazzam's theory of perception is described in detail: all senses require physical contact, with the sense organ receiving particles from the object (e.g., light rays from the eye). Since these conditions cannot be met in the case of God (who is not a body, not at a distance, etc.), vision of God is impossible.

Key Terms
المسافة
الرؤية
الشرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that proximity helps vision, but the passage says the opposite: direct contact prevents vision.
Correction: The passage states that if the seen object sticks to the eye, vision ceases, so distance is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the seen object touches the organ of vision?
A) Vision becomes clearer
B) Vision ceases
C) Vision requires a ray
D) Vision becomes possible by contact

Key Takeaway
The necessity of distance for vision implies that God, who is not spatially located, cannot be seen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot be seen because He is not at a distance, how does that affect your desire to have a visible, tangible proof of His existence? Are you willing to trust in His closeness without needing to see Him?
?
This lesson argues that physical conditions like distance limit vision. In what area of your life do you impose human conditions on God, expecting Him to act in a certain way? Will you consciously release that expectation today and let God be beyond your conditions?

Al-Nazzam's Theory of Perception by Contact
Al-Nazzam's argument for perception requiring physical contact, applied to all senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's doctrine that all sense perception requires direct physical contact between the sense organ and the object, and how this applies to vision.

Core Idea
For al-Nazzam, seeing is like touching—a ray from the eye must physically contact the object.

Al-Nazzam, the leader of the Mu'tazila, had a unique theory of perception: all senses operate by direct physical contact (mumassa). He compares vision to touch, taste, smell, and hearing, arguing that each requires a physical connection. This theory reinforces the impossibility of seeing God, as He cannot be contacted.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Nazzam's examples
Read the passage describing how touch, taste, smell, and sound are perceived through contact. Note how each sense requires a physical interaction.
Al-Nazzam views smell as particles, sound as compressed air.
2
Identify the pattern: each sense requires contact
Observe that al-Nazzam's argument is analogical: all senses work by contact, so vision must also require contact.
This is a key principle in his theory.
3
Extend to vision via the ray
Al-Nazzam concludes that vision occurs when a ray from the eye connects with the seen object, creating contact. This physical contact is necessary for perception.
Since God is not a physical object, contact is impossible, so vision of God is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Refuting the Mu'tazili Interpretation of Musa's Request
Nasafi reports and refutes the Mu'tazili interpretation with five arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili interpretation of Musa's request and the five arguments that refute it.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili reinterpretation contradicts the Quranic text and Musa's prior knowledge.

Imam Nasafi presents a Mu'tazili claim that Musa asked for a sign, not direct vision, then refutes it with five devastating arguments. This passage is crucial for understanding the Sunni position on ru'ya (vision of Allah) and the importance of literal textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili interpretation
Start by reading the first part of the passage where Nasafi reports the Mu'tazili view that Musa asked for a sign.
Note the names Abu Bakr al-Asamm and Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi.
2
Examine the first three textual arguments
Focus on arguments one to three, which are based on the wording of the verse and the response.
These arguments show the inconsistency of the Mu'tazili interpretation with the literal text.
3
Understand the fourth argument from Musa's prior signs
Read the fourth argument which appeals to Musa's previous miraculous signs.
Consider why asking for another sign would be inappropriate for a prophet.
4
Analyze the fifth argument from the conditional
Study the fifth argument that uses the conditional statement 'if it remains in its place, then you will see Me'.
This argument highlights the logical flaw in the Mu'tazili interpretation.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how together these five arguments decisively refute the Mu'tazili interpretation.
Reflect on the importance of reading Quranic verses in their proper context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافترقت المعتزلة في السؤال على هذه الآية وتأويلها، فزعم بعضهم أن موسى عليه السلام سأل ربه آية يعلمه بها على طريق الضرورة، فكان معنى قوله: أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ أي أرني آية أعلمك بها كما أعلم ما أنظر إليه فيحصل لي العلم بطريق الضرورة، فلا يبقى للشبهة والشكوك مجال، وإليه ذهب أبو بكر الأصم وأبو القاسم الكعبي من جملتهم. وهذا التأويل فاسد من وجوه: أحدها: أنه قال: أَرِنِ أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ولم يقل إليها، ولو كان سأل الآية لقال أنظر إليها لأن عند وجود الآية يكون النظر إليها ثم يحصل بها العلم بالله تعالى بالقلب فيكون ناظرا إلى الآية عالما بالله تعالى لا ناظرا إلى الله تعالى. والثاني: أنه قال: لَن تَرَينِي . ولم يقل لن ترى آيتي. والثالث: أن معنى قوله: أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ لو كان أرني آية لكان قوله: كن تركي أي لن ترى آيتي، فحينئذ يتمكن في كلام الله تعالى خلق لأنه أراه أعظم الآيات حيث جعل الجبل دكا. والرابع أن موسى كان وقف على آيات الله تعالى من قلب العصا حية، وتفجير الماء من الحجر، وفلق البحر، واليد البيضاء، وغير ذلك مما خصه الله تعالى به من الآيات الحسية بحيث يستغني عن طلب آية أخرى، بل استغنى عن ذلك أغبى قومه وأبلد خليقة الله تعالى فيما لكثرتها وتراد فيها في أنفسها، وطلب الآيات بعد ظهور ما به الغنية للعاقل كان من تعنت الكفرة، فلا يجوز مثله من موسى مع اصطفاء الله تعالى إياه برسالته وكلامه وبفضله على عالمي زمانه. والخامس وهو الذي يبين حيرة المعتزلة وعنادهم وبلادة أفهامهم، أن الله تعالى قال: فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَركني فيصير على تقدير كلام هؤلاء الجهلة بالحقائق فإن استقر مكانه فسوف ترى آيتي، ولا يخفي على من له أدنى لب أن هذه الآية ترى عند اندكاك الجبل لا عند استقراره بل عند استقراره تنعدم رؤية الآية. يحققه أنه عليه السلام كان عرفه وسمع كلامه وجعل يناجيه، وليس من دأب العاقل أن يقول لمن سبقت له به معرفة وفاوضه في الكلام وناجاه أن يقول له: عرفني نفسك وأقم لي دلالة ثبوتك ووجودك، ولو قال ذلك لنسب إلى الجنون والحمق.
And the Mu'tazila differed concerning the questioning upon this verse and its interpretation. Some of them claimed that Musa, peace be upon him, asked his Lord for a sign by which he would know Him necessarily, so the meaning of his saying: 'Show me, that I may look at You' is: Show me a sign by which I will know You just as I know what I look at, so that knowledge will be obtained by necessity, and no room remains for doubt and suspicions. This was the view of Abu Bakr al-Asamm and Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi among them. This interpretation is corrupt from several aspects: First: He said: 'Show me, that I may look at You' and did not say 'look at it'. If he had asked for a sign, he would have said 'look at it' because when the sign exists, one looks at it, then through it obtains knowledge of Allah the Exalted in the heart, so he would be looking at the sign and knowing Allah, not looking at Allah. Second: He said: 'You will never see Me' and did not say 'You will never see My sign'. Third: The meaning of his saying: 'that I may look at You' if it had meant 'show me a sign', then His saying 'You will never see Me' would have meant 'You will never see My sign'. Then a contradiction would occur in the speech of Allah the Exalted, because He showed him the greatest of signs when He made the mountain crumble. Fourth: Musa had already witnessed the signs of Allah the Exalted: the turning of the staff into a snake, the gushing of water from the stone, the splitting of the sea, the white hand, and other sensory signs that Allah specifically granted him, such that he was in no need of requesting another sign. Rather, even the most foolish of his people and the most dull of Allah's creation were satisfied with those signs due to their abundance and succession, and requesting signs after the appearance of what suffices is an act of obstinacy of the disbelievers. Such a thing is not permissible from Musa given Allah's choice of him for His message and speech and His favor upon him over the worlds of his time. Fifth, which clarifies the confusion, obstinacy, and dullness of understanding of the Mu'tazila: Allah the Exalted said: 'And if it remains in its place, then you will see Me.' On the interpretation of these ignorant of realities, it would mean: 'If it remains in its place, then you will see My sign.' And it is not hidden from anyone with the least intelligence that this sign (i.e. the crumbing) is seen at the crumbling of the mountain, not at its remaining; rather, at its remaining, the sight of the sign disappears. He confirms this by noting that Musa, peace be upon him, already knew Him, heard His speech, and was conversing with Him. It is not the way of a rational person to say to one whom he already knows and with whom he has conversed: 'Make Yourself known to me and establish for me a proof of Your existence.' If he said that, he would be attributed to madness and foolishness.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage reports and then refutes a Mu'tazili interpretation of Musa's request 'Show me, that I may look at You.' The Mu'tazila, specifically Abu Bakr al-Asamm and Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi, claimed that Musa did not ask to see Allah with physical sight, but rather asked for a sign (aya) that would give him knowledge of Allah by necessity, similar to how one knows what he sees. The author declares this interpretation corrupt and provides five arguments. First: The wording of the request uses 'ilayka' (to You), not 'ilayha' (to it). If Musa had asked for a sign, he would have said 'look at it'. Second: The response 'You will never see Me' would have been 'You will never see My sign'. Third: If the request was for a sign, then the response 'You will never see Me' would contradict the fact that Allah did show him a great sign (the mountain crumbling). Fourth: Musa had already seen many miraculous signs, making a request for another sign unnecessary and inappropriate for a prophet. Fifth: The conditional statement 'if it remains in its place, then you will see Me' proves that the vision is of Allah Himself, not a sign, because the sign (the crumbling) occurs when the mountain crumbles, not when it remains. The author adds that Musa already knew Allah intimately through speech and conversation, so asking for a proof of existence would be absurd.

Key Terms
تأويل
آية
رؤية
المعتزلة
فساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the fourth argument against the Mu'tazili interpretation?
A) The word 'ilayka' is used instead of 'ilayha'.
B) Musa already had sufficient signs.
C) The response says 'You will never see My sign'.
D) The conditional statement proves vision.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili interpretation of Musa's request is invalid because it contradicts the literal text, the response, and the context of Musa's prior knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazili interpretation, did you feel a pull to accept it because it seemed more 'rational'? What does that reveal about your trust in the plain meaning of Allah's words? Are you willing to submit to what Allah says, even if your mind struggles?
?
Musa had already seen many miracles and spoken with Allah directly, yet he still longed to see Him. Do you have that same yearning to encounter Allah personally? Or are you content with secondhand knowledge? What has your pursuit of Allah looked like this past month—has it been academic or heartfelt?
?
If someone today reinterpreted a clear command of the Quran because they found it 'problematic,' would you defend the literal meaning as strongly as this passage does? What does your answer say about your attitude towards the integrity of divine revelation?

Refutation of al-Jubba'i's Interpretation of Musa's Request
Nasafi refutes al-Jubba'i's claim that Musa sought to demonstrate impossibility of vision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jubba'i's reinterpretation of Musa's request for vision is invalid based on the Qur'anic wording and the principle of preserving literal meaning.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's wording refutes reinterpretation: Musa asked for himself, not for his people.

Some theologians, like al-Jubba'i, attempted to reconcile the verse where Musa asks to see Allah with the belief that Allah cannot be seen. They claimed Musa knew this and asked only to prove impossibility to his people. However, as Imam al-Nasafi demonstrates, this interpretation contradicts the direct wording of the Qur'an. This lesson examines the refutation and its broader implications for scriptural interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Jubba'i's view
Read the initial claim that Musa asked for vision to show his people that Allah is not visible. Note the reasoning behind this reinterpretation.
2
Examine Nasafi's linguistic refutation
Nasafi points out that Allah's response uses 'Show Me' and 'You will not see Me' – addressing Musa personally, not his people. If the aim were to teach the people, the wording would have been different.
This is a key argument from the Qur'an's own phrasing.
3
Consider the absurdity of analogous reinterpretations
Nasafi shows that if such reinterpretation were allowed, one could change other clear historical narratives in the Qur'an. This highlights the danger of departing from the apparent meaning without strong evidence.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the principle
The lesson concludes that preserving the literal meaning of the Qur'an is essential unless there is compelling reason to interpret otherwise. Al-Jubba'i's view is rejected because it contradicts the clear wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم بعضهم أن موسى عليه السلام كان عالما أن الله تعالى لا يرى ولكن كان قومه يطلبون منه أن يريهم ربهم على ما أخبر الله تعالى عنهم بقوله: ﴿ وَإِذْ قلْتُمْ يَمُوسَى لَن نُّؤْمِنَ لَكَ حَتَّى نَرَى اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً ﴾ فطلب من الله الرؤية ليبين الله تعالى أنه ليس بمرئي ليكون ذلك أشد تمكنا في قلوبهم إلى هذا الاعتراض ذهب الجبائي ومن ساعده.
وهذا أيضا فاسد فإن الله تعالى أخبر أنه قال : أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ثم قال له: لَن تَركني ) ولو كان الأمر على ما زعموا لكان من حق الكلام أن يقول: أرهم ينظروا إليك ثم يقول لهم: لن تروني، فإذا هذا عدول عن إخبار الله تعالى وصرف له إلى غير ما أخبر ، ولو جاز ذا لجاز (۲) أن يقال : إن الله تعالى وإن أخبر عن نوح أنه ركب السفينة كان المراد منه إدريس، وأن الباني للكعبة هو إسحاق مع يعقوب وإن كان الله تعالى قال : يَرْفَعُ إِبْرَاهِمُ الْقَوَاعِدَ مِنَ الْبَيْتِ وَإِسْمَعِيل (۳) الآية ولو قيل ذا لم يخف كفر هذا القائل وتكذيبه الله تعالى في إخباره، فكذا هذا.
And some claimed that Moses (peace be upon him) knew that Allah Almighty cannot be seen, but his people were asking him to show them their Lord, as Allah informed about them in His saying: 'And when you said, O Moses, we will not believe you until we see Allah plainly' (2:55). So he asked Allah for vision so that Allah Almighty would show that He is not visible, so that this would be more firmly rooted in their hearts. This objection was held by al-Jubba'i and those who agreed with him.
And this too is corrupt, for Allah Almighty informed that He said: 'Show me, that I may look at You' then said to him: 'You will not see Me.' If the matter were as they claimed, it would be proper for the speech to say: 'Show them, that they may look at You' then say to them: 'You will not see Me.' So this is a deviation from the information of Allah Almighty and a turning of it to other than what He informed. And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to say that although Allah informed about Noah that he boarded the ark, what was meant was Idris, and that the builder of the Kaaba was Isaac with Jacob even though Allah said: 'And Abraham raised the foundations of the House, and Ishmael' (2:127). If this were said, the disbelief of that speaker and his denial of Allah's information would not be hidden. So likewise this.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage first presents al-Jubba'i's view: that Musa knew Allah cannot be seen but asked for vision to demonstrate to his people that Allah is not visible, thereby strengthening their belief. Then the author refutes it: If that were Musa's intent, Allah would have said 'Show them' not 'Show Me.' The Qur'an clearly reports that Musa asked for himself. Changing the meaning would lead to absurd reinterpretations of other clear texts, such as claiming Noah's ark means Idris or that the Kaaba was built by Isaac and Jacob instead of Abraham and Ishmael—which is clear disbelief. Therefore, al-Jubba'i's interpretation is corrupt.

Key Terms
الجبائي
الرؤية
عدول عن الإخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Musa asked to see Allah not out of personal desire but to prove to his people that Allah cannot be seen.
Correction: The Qur'an's wording shows the request was personal ('Show me'), not for the people, as Allah responds directly to Musa.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute al-Jubba'i's view?
A) By showing that Musa's people were not present.
B) By pointing out that Allah addressed Musa personally.
C) By arguing that the mountain never crumbled.
D) By citing a different verse.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's interpretation is invalid because it contradicts the direct wording of the Qur'an and would lead to unacceptable reinterpretations of other clear texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'an that is clear in its wording, do you trust it literally, or do you sometimes try to reinterpret it to fit your own preconceptions? What area of your belief is most vulnerable to this tendency?
?

?
The reductio ad absurdum exposes that tampering with clear texts leads to disbelief. Are you willing to accept all clear texts of the Qur'an as they are, even if your intellect struggles? What would it cost you to fully surrender in that area?

Al-Nazzam's Unusual View on Accidents
Al-Nazzam's reduction of accidents and his contradiction on sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's claim that motion is the only accident and that colors, tastes, and smells are bodies.

Core Idea
Al-Nazzam reduced all accidents except motion to bodies.

The Mu'tazili scholar al-Nazzam held a unique metaphysical position: he only accepted motion as a true accident. All other sensible qualities, like colors and sounds, he considered to be bodies. This lesson examines his view and the inconsistency in his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Nazzam's position
The passage states al-Nazzam's view that motion alone is an accident, and colors etc. are bodies. Notice his contradiction regarding sounds.
2
Analyze the contradiction
He considered sounds as bodies but did not allow them to be seen. Ask why this is contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن النظام من جملة المعتزلة لم يثبت عرضا إلا الحركة، فأما ما وراء الحركة من الموجودات كالألوان والطعوم والروائح فقد جعلها كلها أجساما وجعل الألوان مرئية لكونها أجساما عنده، فإن لم تكن قائمة بالذات ثم ناقض، ولم يجوز رؤية الأصوات وإن كانت أجساما فأما المتقدمون من أصحابنا فقد اختلفوا في جواز درك الأعراض بالحواس الأخر نحو الأصوات أنها هل تدرك بحاسة السمع، فطرد عبد الله بن سعيد القطان الكلام في كل ذلك وقال : لا يجوز إدراك عرض ما بحاسة من الحواس إذ لو أدرك لما جرى الخلاف في ثبوتها بين أرباب الحواس السليمة، وزعم أن المسموع هو الذات الذي قام به الكلام والصوت لا الكلام والصوت، فأما من سواه من أصحابنا الذين لا يجوزون رؤية الأعراض فإنهم يجوزون
Then al-Nazzam, from among the Mu'tazila, did not affirm an accident except motion. As for existents other than motion, like colors, tastes, and smells, he made them all bodies, and he made colors visible because they are bodies according to him. If they are not self-subsistent, then he contradicted, and he did not permit seeing sounds even though they are bodies. As for the early ones among our associates, they differed regarding the permissibility of perceiving accidents by other senses, like sounds: are they perceived by the sense of hearing? Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan extended the discourse in all that and said: It is not permissible to perceive any accident by any sense, for if it were perceived, there would be no disagreement about its existence among people with sound senses. And he claimed that what is heard is the substance (dhat) in which speech and sound inhere, not the speech and sound itself. As for others among our associates who do not permit seeing accidents, they permit [perceiving them]...
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage introduces al-Nazzam's unique position among the Mu'tazila: he only accepted motion as an accident, and considered other sensible qualities like colors, tastes, and smells to be bodies. Hence colors are visible because they are bodies. However, he contradicted himself by not allowing sounds to be seen even though he considered them bodies. The passage then discusses the early Maturidi scholars who differed on whether accidents can be perceived by the senses other than sight. Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan held a strict position: no accident can be perceived by any sense, using the same argument as before (if perceived, there would be no disagreement). He claimed that what is heard is the substance that bears speech and sound, not the speech/sound itself. Others among the Maturidis, while denying seeing accidents, allowed perceiving them (e.g., hearing sounds). The text cuts off before completing their view.

Key Terms
النظام
أعراض
حركة
أجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume all Mu'tazila agreed on the nature of accidents.
Correction: Al-Nazzam held a minority view, considering only motion as an accident.

Checkpoint
What did al-Nazzam consider to be the only true accident?
A) Colors
B) Motion
C) Sounds
D) All qualities

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's position is that motion is the sole accident, while all other sensible qualities are bodies, leading to a contradiction regarding sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nazzam's inconsistency arose from clinging to a reductionist principle. Where in your own understanding of Islam have you accepted a contradiction because you were attached to a particular view? Commit to honestly examining one such area this week.
?

?
Is there a standard you apply to some situations but not to similar ones—whether in your practice of Islam, your treatment of others, or your reasoning? Identify one such double standard and write a concrete step to eliminate it.

Maturidi Disagreement on Perceiving Accidents by Senses
Maturidi disagreement on sense perception of accidents, especially sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early Maturidi difference on whether accidents can be perceived by senses like hearing, and Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan's strict position.

Core Idea
Maturidi scholars disagreed on whether senses perceive accidents or substances.

The early Maturidi scholars, while generally denying seeing accidents, differed on whether other senses can perceive them. Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan argued that no accident can be perceived by any sense, using a consistency argument. This lesson explores his reasoning and the contrasting view of other Maturidis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disagreement
The passage notes that early Maturidis differed on perceiving accidents by other senses, specifically sounds. Read carefully to see two positions.
2

He says if an accident were perceived, there would be no disagreement among sound-sensed people. He claims what is heard is the substance, not the sound.
3
Contrast with other Maturidis
Other Maturidis who deny seeing accidents allow perceiving them (e.g., hearing). The passage cuts off before completing their view.

Al-Nazzam's View on Accidents and Visibility
Al-Nazzam's view that only motion is an accident and the resulting contradiction on visibility of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's distinctive position within the Mu'tazila that only motion is an accident, and how his view on visibility leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Al-Nazzam's system breaks: sounds are bodies but not visible.

The Mu'tazili theologian al-Nazzam held a highly unusual view that only motion qualifies as an accident. In this passage, the author summarizes al-Nazzam's position and points out an internal inconsistency regarding the visibility of bodies. By examining his claims, we will see how theological arguments must be internally consistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to understand al-Nazzam's claims about accidents and bodies.
Pay attention to the contrast between colors and sounds.
2
Identify the contradiction
Notice that al-Nazzam makes colors visible because they are bodies, but denies visibility of sounds even though he also considers them bodies. This is the key contradiction.
3
Consider the implication
Reflect on why this inconsistency matters for his overall theory and how it serves as a refutation point in the broader discussion.
This passage is part of a larger argument against al-Nazzam's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن النظام من جملة المعتزلة لم يثبت عرضا إلا الحركة، فأما ما وراء الحركة من الموجودات كالألوان والطعوم والروائح فقد جعلها كلها أجساما وجعل الألوان مرئية لكونها أجساما عنده، فإن لم تكن قائمة بالذات ثم ناقض، ولم يجوز رؤية الأصوات وإن كانت أجساما
Then al-Nazzam, among the Mu'tazila, did not affirm any accident except motion. As for existents beyond motion, such as colors, tastes, and smells, he made them all bodies and made colors visible because they are bodies according to him, though they are not self-subsistent; then he contradicted himself, and did not allow the seeing of sounds even though they are bodies.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage presents the view of al-Nazzam, a prominent Mu'tazili theologian. He held a unique position that only motion is a true accident; other sensible qualities like colors, tastes, and smells are not accidents but bodies. For him, colors are visible because they are bodies, even though they are not self-subsistent. However, the author points out a contradiction: al-Nazzam also considers sounds to be bodies (since they are not motion), but he does not allow seeing sounds. So if visibility is tied to being a body, sounds should also be visible, yet he denies it. This reveals an inconsistency in al-Nazzam's system. The author is setting up a target for refutation later.

Correction: The passage highlights that if visibility is based on being a body, then sounds should also be visible, but al-Nazzam denies it, showing inconsistency.

Al-Nazzam's restriction of accidents to motion led to an inconsistency regarding the visibility of sounds, which undermines his theory.

Are you willing to examine them honestly, even if it challenges your comfort? Commit to studying one such principle with a critical mind this week.
?

Disagreement on Perceiving Accidents by Senses
A note on the internal dispute regarding perception of accidents by senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal disagreement among early Maturidi scholars about whether accidents can be perceived by senses other than sight, and how this limits the universal application of the argument from disagreement.

Core Idea
The proof against perceiving accidents by senses is contested within the school.

This passage reveals an important internal debate within the author's own school. While some scholars used the disagreement among rational people to argue that accidents cannot be seen, 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan extended this reasoning to all senses. However, most of the author's companions accepted perception of accidents by the appropriate senses, thereby limiting the force of that proof. Understanding this nuance prevents oversimplification of the school's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Note the key figures and their arguments.
The passage is dense; read twice.
2
Identify the two positions
First, find al-Qattan's strict view that no accident is perceptible by any sense. Then, identify the majority view that accidents are perceptible by their appropriate senses.
Al-Qattan extends the argument from disagreement to all senses.
3
Understand the reasoning
Al-Qattan argues that if accidents were perceived, there would be no disagreement. The majority rejects this extension, noting that they allow perception of certain accidents.
The disagreement argument only works if one denies all sensory perception of accidents.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this internal dispute limits the universal application of the disagreement argument. The author's school does not uniformly accept it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما المتقدمون من أصحابنا فقد اختلفوا في جواز درك الأعراض بالحواس الأخر نحو الأصوات أنها هل تدرك بحاسة السمع، فطرد عبد الله بن سعيد القطان الكلام في كل ذلك وقال : لا يجوز إدراك عرض ما بحاسة من الحواس إذ لو أدرك لما جرى الخلاف في ثبوتها بين أرباب الحواس السليمة، وزعم أن المسموع هو الذات الذي قام به الكلام والصوت لا الكلام والصوت، فأما من سواه من أصحابنا الذين لا يجوزون رؤية الأعراض فإنهم يجوزون استدراكهما بسائر الحواس الموضوعة لدرك كل نوع منها فلم يطرد اعتلالهم في إخراج الأعراض من أن تكو مرئية بجريان الخلاف في ثبوتها بين العقلاء
As for the early ones among our companions, they disagreed about the permissibility of perceiving accidents by the other senses, such as sounds — whether they are perceived by the sense of hearing. So 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan extended the argument in all of that and said: It is not permissible to perceive any accident by any sense, because if it were perceived, there would be no disagreement about their existence among people with sound senses. He claimed that what is heard is the essence (dhat) which is subsistent by speech and sound, not the speech and sound themselves. As for those other than him among our companions who do not allow seeing accidents, they allow perceiving them by the other senses that are placed for perceiving each type, so their argument for excluding accidents from being visible due to the occurrence of disagreement about their existence among rational people does not hold.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage discusses the internal disagreement within the author's own school (the Maturidis or early Hanafis). The opponent, 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, took a strict position: no accident whatsoever can be perceived by any sense, arguing by the same proof from disagreement as earlier. However, the majority of the author's companions disagree: they allow perception of accidents by the appropriate senses (e.g., sounds by hearing). For them, the argument from disagreement does not apply consistently because they accept that some accidents are perceptible. Thus, the earlier argument that accidents are invisible due to disagreement is not universal; it only works for those who hold that no accident is perceived by any sense. The author notes that this limits the force of that proof.

Key Terms
المتقدمون من أصحابنا (the early ones among our companions)
عبد الله بن سعيد القطان ('Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan)
إدراك (idrak, perception)
حواس (hawass)
ذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument from disagreement (that accidents cannot be perceived because people disagree about their existence) is universally accepted by the author's school.
Correction: The passage shows that this argument is contested within the school; al-Qattan applies it to all senses, but the majority rejects its universal application, accepting perception of accidents by appropriate senses.

Checkpoint
According to 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, why can no accident be perceived?
A) Because accidents are not real
B) Because if they were perceived, there would be no disagreement about them
C) Because the senses are unreliable
D) Because only bodies are perceptible

Key Takeaway
The proof that accidents are not perceived due to disagreement is not universally accepted; some scholars limit its scope, showing the diversity within the school.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that even within a single theological school, scholars deeply disagreed about something as basic as how we perceive sound. When you hear the Qur'an recited, do you assume that what you hear is the eternal speech of Allah or an accident attached to a created voice? How does your answer affect your certainty that you have truly heard Allah's word? What is at stake if you are wrong?
?
Al-Qattan argued that if accidents were perceptible, rational people would not disagree about them. But the majority rejected this, acknowledging that rational people do disagree. Think of a time when you were absolutely certain about something because of your senses—only to later discover that others perceived the same event differently. Did that shake your trust in your senses? How do you reconcile such disagreements with your reliance on sensory experience for knowledge of the world and of religion?
?
The scholars here used the fact of disagreement as a proof for their positions. But disagreement itself is a reality in theology. This lesson shows that even proofs based on disagreement are themselves disputed. Would you be willing to hold your own theological beliefs with less certainty, knowing that the very arguments you rely on are contested even within your own tradition? What would it cost you to admit that some of your convictions might be only probable, not certain?

Refuting al-Nazzam: Colors as Accidents
The author begins refuting al-Nazzam's claim that colors are bodies and visibility depends on corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nazzam's claim that colors are bodies is false and how the definition of accident applies to colors.

Core Idea
Colors cannot be bodies because they depend on a locus.

Al-Nazzam built his theory of visibility on two unsound principles. The first is that colors are bodies. The author of this text begins his refutation by arguing that colors are actually accidents, not bodies. This lesson examines that refutation and its foundation in the definition of an accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Nazzam's First Principle
Read the passage and note that al-Nazzam's first principle is the claim that colors are bodies. This is essential to his argument about visibility.
The text identifies this as one of two unsound principles.
2
Understand the Refutation
The author refutes by stating that colors are accidents, not bodies. Look for the evidence: the definition of accident applies to colors.
The definition includes inability to subsist alone, need for a locus, and impermanence.
3
Apply the Definition of Accident
Consider how motion is accepted as an accident by consensus, and then see the same logic applied to colors. This shows colors fit the definition.
The text is incomplete, but the direction is clear.

Proof and Refutation on Visibility of Substances
Nasafi presents consensus and distinction-based proof for visibility of substances, then refutes deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses consensus and the argument from discrimination to prove that substances are visible, and how he refutes opponents who deny this by showing the inconsistency in their position.

Core Idea
Consensus and discrimination prove visibility of substances; opponents are inconsistent.

Nasafi presents a dual proof for the visibility of substances: first, the consensus of all Mu'tazila, and second, a logical argument based on discrimination by sight. He then turns to refute those who deny the visibility of substances but affirm visibility of colors, showing that their reasoning is inconsistent. This passage is a classic example of kalam dialectic, using both consensus and rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first proof: consensus
Read the opening line where Nasafi states that the agreement of all Mu'tazila suffices for proof. Consider why consensus is a valid argument in this context.
Notice that this consensus spans the entire Mu'tazila school, which is a strong claim.
2
Understand the second proof: discrimination
Read the second sentence: discrimination between genera of substances by sight is like discrimination between colors. This is an inductive argument from analogy.
Think about what 'discrimination' means here – it is the ability to distinguish different types by visual perception.
3
See how the refutation works: consistency challenge
In the third sentence, Nasafi challenges the opponent: if you accept colors as visible because of discrimination, then the same applies to substances, modalities, etc. Ask yourself: is this a valid logical move?
The opponent cannot arbitrarily restrict visibility without giving a principled reason.
4
Apply to specific opponents: al-Nazzam and Abu Hashim
Read how Nasafi targets al-Nazzam and Abu Hashim separately. For al-Nazzam, the argument from discrimination forces visibility of motion; for Abu Hashim, the demand for a differentiating cause is used.
These are specific historical figures with known positions. Understanding their views adds depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن الجواهر مرئية موافقة جميع المعتزلة إيانا على ذلك فيغنينا الإجماع عن إقامة البرهان على ذلك ، ثم الدليل على ذلك حصول التمييز بين أجناس الجواهر بالرؤية كحصوله بين أنواع الألوان. ثم يقال لهذا الذي لا يجوز رؤية الجواهر ولم يجوز إلا رؤية الألوان: بم عرفت أن الألوان مرئية ؟ فإن قال بالتمييز بين أنواعها بالرؤية فكذا يلزم في الجواهر والأكوان والطول والقصر والحركة والسكون وغير ذلك. وبعين هذا يطالب النظام فيقال له : لم قلت إن الألوان مرئية؟ فلا يمكن أن يتعلق بالتمييز بين أنواعها فيلزم رؤية الحركة والسكون ، وكذا أبو هاشم يطالب بالعلة التي لأجلها جعل الألوان مرئية ثم يطالب بالتفرقة بينها وبين الأكوان ويسوي بينها وبينها بعين تلك العلة.
The proof that substances are visible is the agreement of all the Mu'tazila with us on that, so the consensus suffices us from establishing a demonstration for that. Then the proof for that is the occurrence of discrimination between genera of substances by sight, like the occurrence of discrimination between types of colors. Then one says to the one who does not allow the visibility of substances and does not allow except the visibility of colors: How did you know that colors are visible? If he says: by discrimination between their types by sight, then the same applies to substances, modalities, length, shortness, movement, stillness, and other things. And by the same [argument] al-Nazzam is called to account, and it is said to him: Why did you say that colors are visible? For he cannot rely on discrimination between their types, because that would necessitate the visibility of movement and stillness. Likewise, Abu Hashim is required to give the cause for which he made colors visible, and then he is required to differentiate between them and modalities, and one equates them with them by that same cause.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation passage. It first offers a second proof for the visibility of substances: the consensus of all Mu'tazila, which suffices without further argument. Then it adds a logical proof: we discriminate between genera of substances by sight just as we do between colors. The author then shifts to a dialectical refutation (objection-and-answer) against those who deny visibility of substances but affirm visibility of colors. The refutation uses the principle of consistency: if they accept colors as visible because of visual discrimination, then the same method forces them to accept substances, modalities, etc. as visible. The author specifically challenges al-Nazzam and Abu Hashim, showing that their positions are incoherent. Al-Nazzam, who considered colors bodies, cannot escape because discrimination applies equally. Abu Hashim is asked for a differentiating cause between colors and modalities, but if he admits colors visible by some cause, the same cause would apply to modalities. The argument demonstrates that one cannot arbitrarily restrict visibility to certain categories.

العلة

Misconception: One might think that consensus alone proves visibility without further rational argument.
Correction: Nasafi adds the discrimination argument as a second, independent proof, showing that consensus is not the sole basis.

Checkpoint

C) They deny existence as cause
D) They are ignorant of the text

Key Takeaway
The visibility of substances is proven by both consensus and rational inference from discrimination, and any attempt to deny it leads to inconsistency when applied consistently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refutation of al-Nazzam's Claim that Rays are a Body
The author refutes al-Nazzam's claim that rays are a body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the kalam argument refutes the notion that the ray of vision is a body, based on the nature of accidents and the impossibility of instant travel.

Core Idea
A ray cannot be a body because bodies cannot travel instantaneously across vast distances.

This passage tackles a specific claim by the Mu'tazili theologian al-Nazzam, who argued that vision works through a physical ray emanating from the eye. The author demonstrates the impossibility of this view using rational arguments based on the nature of bodies and accidents. Let's examine how the argument proceeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of arguments.
The passage is dense with technical terms; don't worry if not everything is clear yet.
2
Identify the main claim and its refutation
Notice that the author first states al-Nazzam's claim is invalid, then defines the ray as an accident (brightness in sight).
The key is understanding why accidents cannot transfer.
3
Analyze the first argument: instantaneous travel
The author argues that if the ray were a body, it could not travel instantly from the eye to distant stars. We see stars immediately, so the ray cannot be a body.
Consider the speed of light vs. physical travel.
4
Analyze the second argument: tactile knowledge
If vision were via touch, then seeing an object would produce knowledge of its heat, texture, etc., which does not happen. Therefore, vision is not touch.
This shows that the ray cannot be a body that touches objects.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The ray is an accident (brightness) and not a body. The arguments show the impossibility of al-Nazzam's position.
This strengthens the Ash'ari and Maturidi position that vision occurs without a physical ray.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم دعوى النظام اتصال الشعاع وكونه جسما فاسد، لأن الشعاع هو الانجلاء القائم بالبصر، وهو عرض لدخوله تحت حيز الأعراض، والأعراض لا مماسة لها ولا انتقال من محل إلى محل فلا يتصور انتقاله من العين إلى المرئي على أن الشعاع لو كان جسما لكان لا يتصور ما يقول من حيث إن الإنسان يرى السماء وما فيها من الكواكب كلما فتح عينيه من غير تخلل مدة مع بعد ما بينهما، ولا يتصور انتقال جسم في هذه المدة من الأرض إلى السماء مع بعد ما بينهما من المسافة، وكذا لو حصل العلم بالرؤية بطريق المماسة لحصل ما هو المخصوص بالمماسة من العلم بحرارة الممسوس وبرودته ولينه وخشونته ورخاوته وصلابته بمجرد الرؤية، لأن العلم المخصوص بالمماسة لا ينعدم عند وجودها وإن حصل علم آخر وراءه كما في الذوق، فإن العلم المختص بها كذا هذا.
Then al-Nazzam's claim of the connection of the ray and that it is a body is invalid. Because the ray is the brightness subsisting in sight, and it is an accident because it falls under the category of accidents. Accidents have no touch and no transfer from one locus to another, so its transfer from the eye to the seen is inconceivable. Moreover, if the ray were a body, then what he says would be inconceivable in that man sees the sky and what is in it of stars whenever he opens his eyes without any time elapsing, despite the distance between them. It is inconceivable for a body to transfer in this time from the earth to the sky, given the distance of the space between them. And likewise, if knowledge of vision occurred by way of touch, then what is specific to touch—knowledge of the heat, cold, softness, roughness, softness, and hardness of the touched thing—would occur merely by vision, because the knowledge specific to touch is not absent when it exists, even if another knowledge occurs beyond it, as in taste. For the knowledge specific to it is like this.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Nazzam's claim that the ray is a body that connects the seer to the seen. The author argues that the ray is actually an accident (the brightness in the eye), and accidents cannot transfer from place to place. If the ray were a body, then seeing distant objects like the sky and stars instantly would be impossible, because a body cannot travel such a distance instantaneously. Additionally, if vision were via touch, then seeing an object would immediately give tactile knowledge (heat, cold, texture), which does not happen. This shows that touch is not the mechanism of vision.
