Al-Nazzam's Theory of Sarfah
Al-Nasafi presents al-Nazzam's argument for sarfah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's argument that the Qur'an's inimitability is due to divine prevention (sarfah) rather than an intrinsic quality of the text.

Core Idea
Al-Nazzam denied the Qur'an's textual inimitability, claiming God simply turned away challengers.

Al-Nazzam, a prominent Mu'tazili theologian, proposed a controversial theory about the Qur'an's miracle. His argument challenges the very foundation of i'jaz. Understanding his reasoning is essential before examining the Sunni refutation. Let's explore his claims as presented by al-Nasafi.

Guided Path
1
Read the presentation of al-Nazzam's view
Read the Arabic passage from 'وما يزعم' to 'مقدور في نفسه' along with its translation. Pay attention to his central claim.
Note that he begins by denying the Qur'an is inimitable in composition.
2
Trace his proof from parts to whole
Identify his argument: if individual parts (like single words) are not inimitable, the whole cannot be either. See how he applies this to verses and surahs.
Compare to his analogy of tawatur and ijma'.
3
Understand the beard analogy
Read his illustration of a man grabbing his beard and challenging others. Understand why he considers this a valid analogy for sarfah.
This analogy clarifies his view that the miracle is external.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this theory implies about the Qur'an's linguistic excellence and the nature of prophethood.
Classical Sunni scholars firmly rejected this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعم النظام أن القرآن ليس بمعجوز النظم وأن كل معجم متبلد يقدر أن يأتي بمثل نظمه العجيب ووصفه البديع، واحتجاجه لذلك أنه متى يعجز الإنسان أعند قوله: الحمد لله أم عند قوله: رب العالمين أم عند قوله: الرحمن الرحيم، وادعاء العجز عند كل من ذلك باطل لما أن ما دون سورة ليس بمعجز عندكم، ثم إذا لم يكن أفراد الآيات معجوزاً عنها ، فكذا المجموع منها إذ حكم الجمل لا يخالف حكم الأفراد، ولهذا زعم أن المتواتر من الأخبار محتمل للكذب لأن خبر كل فرد من أهل التواتر محتمل للكذب فكذا عند الاجتماع، وأن الخطأ على الإجماع جائز لجوازه على كل فرد من المجتمعين، وإنما الإعجاز حصل بالصرفة ومعناها: أنه تعالى صرف همتهم بلطفه عن الاشتغال بمعارضته أنه ممكن، ومثل هذا مع معجز لما فيه من نقض العادة حتى إن من جعل دليل رسالته أن يقبض على لحية وهم لا يقبضون على ذلك، ثم إنه قبض على لحيته وتحدى الناس إلى ذلك فلم يقبض واحد منهم على لحيته كان ذلك معجزاً لما فيه من صرف هممهم عن الأمر الممكن لا أن ذلك ليس في مقدور في نفسه.

هذا منه كلام باطل واحتجاج فاسد، فإن أقرب ما يجاب عنه أن يقال: ائت بقصيدة مثل قصيدة امرئ القيس: قفا نبك، أو مثل قصيدته: ألا أنعم صباحًا أيها الطلل البالي، أو مثل قصيدته خليلي مرا بي على أم جندب أو مثل قصيدته: شمالك شرق بعدما كان أقصر، وغيرها من القصائد له ولغيره من فحول الشعراء كالنابغة وطرفه وزهير والأعشى ليظهر عند عجزه عن ذلك أنه مكابر فيما يقول، ويقال له : متى عجزت أنت أعند التكلم بالكلمة الأولى أو عند التكلم بالكلمة
And what al-Nazzam claims that the Qur'an is not inimitable in composition, and that every mute and dull person can produce the like of its wondrous composition and wonderful description. And his proof for that is that when does a person become incapable? At His saying 'Alhamdulillah' or at His saying 'Rabbil Alameen' or at His saying 'Ar-Rahman Ar-Raheem'? And the claim of incapacity at each of that is false, because that which is less than a surah is not miraculous according to you. Then if the individual verses are not incapable of being imitated, then so is the whole, because the ruling of composites does not differ from the ruling of individuals. For this reason he claimed that mutawatir reports are possible to be false, because the report of each individual of the people of tawatur is possible to be false, so too when they gather. And that error in consensus is permissible because it is permissible from each individual of those gathered. And that the miracle only occurred through sarfah, and its meaning is that God Most High turned away their ambition by His subtlety from occupying themselves with opposing it, since it is possible. And such a thing is a miracle because it contains a breaking of custom, to the point that if someone made his proof of his prophethood that he should seize a beard and that they do not seize that, then he seized his beard and challenged the people to that, and not one of them seized his beard, that would be a miracle because it involves turning their ambitions from a possible matter, not because that matter is not within capacity in itself.

This from him is false speech and corrupt argument. For the closest answer to it is to say: Bring a poem like the poem of Imru' al-Qays: 'Qifa nabki', or like his poem: 'Ala asbaha ayyuha al-talalu al-bali', or like his poem 'Khulayya murra bi bi ala Ummi Jundub' or like his poem 'Shimaluka sharqun ba'da ma kana aqsar', and other poems of his and of other master poets like al-Nabigha, Tarafa, Zuhayr, and al-A'sha, so that when you are incapable of that it becomes clear that you are obstinate in what you say. And it is said to him: When are you incapable? At the utterance of the first word or at the utterance of the word...
Source pages p. 750

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Nazzam's theory that the Qur'an's inimitability is not due to its text but to divine intervention (sarfah). First, it presents al-Nazzam's argument: He claims that the Qur'an is not intrinsically inimitable in composition, because if one considers each part (e.g., a single verse, words like 'Alhamdulillah'), none is impossible to imitate. The whole is just the sum of parts. He analogizes to tawatur (multiple transmission) – if each individual report could be false, the collective could also be false – and to ijma' (consensus) – if each individual can err, the collective can err. Therefore, the miracle is only the external act of God turning away the opponents' ambition (sarfah). He illustrates with an analogy of someone grabbing his own beard challenging others to do the same, and they fail, which is a miracle due to the breaking of custom.

Then the refutation begins: This is false speech. The simplest response is to challenge al-Nazzam to produce a poem comparable to the great Arab poets like Imru' al-Qays, al-Nabigha, etc. If he cannot, then his claim that producing similar is possible is proven false. The argument points out that his own logic about parts and wholes would also apply to poetry. The refutation is incomplete in this passage, as the text cuts off.

Key Terms
نظام
صرفة
إعجاز
نظم
تواتر
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sarfah means the Qur'an is not a miracle at all.
Correction: Al-Nazzam still considers the Qur'an a miracle, but one caused by divine intervention preventing imitation, not by its intrinsic linguistic quality.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nazzam, what is the basis of the Qur'an's miracle?
A) Its eloquent composition
B) The divine turning away of opponents (sarfah)
C) The prophethood of Muhammad
D) The consensus of the community

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam reduces the Qur'an's miracle to an external act of prevention, stripping the text of intrinsic inimitability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you base your conviction on its beautiful language and message, or on the fact that no one can produce its like? If al-Nazzam is right that the miracle is only external prevention, would your faith be shaken? What would that reveal about the foundation of your belief? Commit to examining your reliance on the miraculous nature of the Qur'an's text versus its content.
?
Al-Nazzam compares the miracle to someone challenging others to grab his beard and no one being able to. Does this analogy seem trivial or even offensive to the grandeur of the Qur'an? Does your emotional reaction reveal a deeper assumption about what a miracle 'should' be? Will you reflect on whether you are more impressed by dramatic displays or by the subtle divine intervention in daily life?
?
If you accept that God could turn away opponents from imitating the Qur'an, then what prevents you from turning away your own disobedience? Could it be that your own neglect of the Qur'an is a sign that God has not 'turned away' your heart? Ask yourself: am I relying on God's subtle turning to keep me on the path, or am I actively engaging with the Qur'an? Resolve to read and ponder this passage until it stirs you to action.

Refutation of al-Nazzam: The Poetic Challenge
Al-Nasafi refutes al-Nazzam by challenging him to produce poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial stage of al-Nasafi's refutation, which relies on a practical challenge to al-Nazzam's theory.

Core Idea
The simplest refutation: produce poetry like the ancients if the Qur'an is not inimitable.

Al-Nasafi begins his response by dismissing al-Nazzam's argument as false and issuing a direct challenge. He calls on al-Nazzam to imitate the great pre-Islamic poets—a test that would expose the impossibility of producing anything comparable to the Qur'an. This challenge cleverly turns al-Nazzam's own logic against him.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read from 'هذا منه كلام باطل' to the cut-off point. Notice the tone and the immediate dismissal.
Al-Nasafi does not entertain the argument; he attacks its foundation.
2
Identify the challenge
Find the names of poets and the specific examples al-Nasafi uses. Why does he choose pre-Islamic poetry?
He knows al-Nazzam would claim any composition could be imitated.
3
Analyze the rhetorical strategy
Consider how this challenge connects to al-Nazzam's own analogy about parts. If al-Nazzam cannot compose a line of poetry, his own theory fails.
This is a practical demonstration of incapacity.
4
Acknowledge the incomplete text
Note that the refutation is cut off. What might al-Nasafi have added next? Reflect on possible completions.
The missing part likely continues the challenge or provides a deeper argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعم النظام أن القرآن ليس بمعجوز النظم وأن كل معجم متبلد يقدر أن يأتي بمثل نظمه العجيب ووصفه البديع، واحتجاجه لذلك أنه متى يعجز الإنسان أعند قوله: الحمد لله أم عند قوله: رب العالمين أم عند قوله: الرحمن الرحيم، وادعاء العجز عند كل من ذلك باطل لما أن ما دون سورة ليس بمعجز عندكم، ثم إذا لم يكن أفراد الآيات معجوزاً عنها ، فكذا المجموع منها إذ حكم الجمل لا يخالف حكم الأفراد، ولهذا زعم أن المتواتر من الأخبار محتمل للكذب لأن خبر كل فرد من أهل التواتر محتمل للكذب فكذا عند الاجتماع، وأن الخطأ على الإجماع جائز لجوازه على كل فرد من المجتمعين، وإنما الإعجاز حصل بالصرفة ومعناها: أنه تعالى صرف همتهم بلطفه عن الاشتغال بمعارضته أنه ممكن، ومثل هذا مع معجز لما فيه من نقض العادة حتى إن من جعل دليل رسالته أن يقبض على لحية وهم لا يقبضون على ذلك، ثم إنه قبض على لحيته وتحدى الناس إلى ذلك فلم يقبض واحد منهم على لحيته كان ذلك معجزاً لما فيه من صرف هممهم عن الأمر الممكن لا أن ذلك ليس في مقدور في نفسه.

هذا منه كلام باطل واحتجاج فاسد، فإن أقرب ما يجاب عنه أن يقال: ائت بقصيدة مثل قصيدة امرئ القيس: قفا نبك، أو مثل قصيدته: ألا أنعم صباحًا أيها الطلل البالي، أو مثل قصيدته خليلي مرا بي على أم جندب أو مثل قصيدته: شمالك شرق بعدما كان أقصر، وغيرها من القصائد له ولغيره من فحول الشعراء كالنابغة وطرفه وزهير والأعشى ليظهر عند عجزه عن ذلك أنه مكابر فيما يقول، ويقال له : متى عجزت أنت أعند التكلم بالكلمة الأولى أو عند التكلم بالكلمة
And what al-Nazzam claims that the Qur'an is not inimitable in composition, and that every mute and dull person can produce the like of its wondrous composition and wonderful description. And his proof for that is that when does a person become incapable? At His saying 'Alhamdulillah' or at His saying 'Rabbil Alameen' or at His saying 'Ar-Rahman Ar-Raheem'? And the claim of incapacity at each of that is false, because that which is less than a surah is not miraculous according to you. Then if the individual verses are not incapable of being imitated, then so is the whole, because the ruling of composites does not differ from the ruling of individuals. For this reason he claimed that mutawatir reports are possible to be false, because the report of each individual of the people of tawatur is possible to be false, so too when they gather. And that error in consensus is permissible because it is permissible from each individual of those gathered. And that the miracle only occurred through sarfah, and its meaning is that God Most High turned away their ambition by His subtlety from occupying themselves with opposing it, since it is possible. And such a thing is a miracle because it contains a breaking of custom, to the point that if someone made his proof of his prophethood that he should seize a beard and that they do not seize that, then he seized his beard and challenged the people to that, and not one of them seized his beard, that would be a miracle because it involves turning their ambitions from a possible matter, not because that matter is not within capacity in itself.

This from him is false speech and corrupt argument. For the closest answer to it is to say: Bring a poem like the poem of Imru' al-Qays: 'Qifa nabki', or like his poem: 'Ala asbaha ayyuha al-talalu al-bali', or like his poem 'Khulayya murra bi bi ala Ummi Jundub' or like his poem 'Shimaluka sharqun ba'da ma kana aqsar', and other poems of his and of other master poets like al-Nabigha, Tarafa, Zuhayr, and al-A'sha, so that when you are incapable of that it becomes clear that you are obstinate in what you say. And it is said to him: When are you incapable? At the utterance of the first word or at the utterance of the word...

?

The Mu'tazila Objection Against Karamat
Mu'tazila objection against karamat with two reasons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that karamat are impossible because they would undermine the distinctiveness of prophetic miracles.

Core Idea
Karamat would erase the line between prophet and saint, says the Mu'tazila.

The Mu'tazila, known for their rationalist theology, denied the possibility of saintly miracles (karamat) on the grounds that they would create confusion with prophetic miracles (mu'jizat). In this passage, we encounter their core argument: that without a clear distinction, the very proof of prophethood collapses. We'll examine why they considered this impossible and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the framing question and the Mu'tazila position. Then read the translation.
Notice that the Mu'tazila start with a question about possibility.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the two distinct reasons the Mu'tazila give for the impossibility of karamat. The first is about confusion with mu'jizat, the second about lack of need.
These reasons are logically connected: the first is based on consequence, the second on purpose.
3
Reflect on the necessity argument
Consider why the Mu'tazila think there is no need for karamat. How does this contrast with the need for prophetic miracles?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إثبات كرامات الأولياء. ومن هذا القبيل كرامات الأولياء أن إظهارها عليهم هل هو في حيز الممكنات أم هو ممتنع؟ استحالة الكرامة عند المعتزلة فزعمت المعتزلة أنه ممتنع لأنه لو جاز لالتبست المعجزة بالكرامة، ولم يبق إلى الوصول إلى العلم بكون النبي نبيًا طريق، وهذا ممتنع، ولأن نقض العادة لفائدة الوصول إلى معرفة النبي والتمييز بينه وبين المتنبي وبالناس إلى ذلك حاجة ماسة، فأما الحاجة إلى معرفة الولي من غير الولي فمنعدمة. وأهل الحق يقولون بثبوتها لورود أخبار كثيرة وحكايات مستفيضة منها استفاضة الخبر عن صاحب سليمان في إتيانه بعرش بلقيس قبل ارتداد الطرف. ومنها رؤية عمر رضي الله عنه على المنبر بالمدينة جيشه بنهاوند حتى قال: يا سارية الجبل وسمع سارية ذلك الصوت على مسافة ذهاب قريب من خمسمائة فرسخ حتى صعد الجبل وأخرج منه الكمين على البدء، وكان ذلك سبب الفتح.
The discussion on establishing the miracles of the saints. And of this kind are the miracles of the saints: is their manifestation upon them within the realm of possibilities or is it impossible? The impossibility of the miracle according to the Mu'tazila: Then the Mu'tazila claimed that it is impossible because if it were possible, the miracle (mu'jiza) would be confused with the saintly miracle (karama), and no way would remain to attain knowledge that a prophet is a prophet, and this is impossible. And because breaking the custom (naqd al-'ada) is for the benefit of attaining knowledge of the prophet and distinguishing between him and the false claimant, and people have a pressing need for that. As for the need to know the saint from the non-saint, it is nonexistent. And the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) affirm its occurrence due to many reports and widespread accounts: among them the widely transmitted report about the companion of Solomon in bringing the throne of Bilqis before the twinkling of an eye. And among them the vision of 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) on the pulpit in Medina of his army in Nahawand until he said: 'O Sariya, the mountain!' And Sariya heard that voice at a distance of nearly five hundred parasangs, so he ascended the mountain and brought out the ambush from it at the beginning, and that was the cause of the victory.
Source pages p. 780

Commentary
This passage presents the debate over the possibility and actual occurrence of karamat (saintly miracles). It begins by framing the question: are such manifestations possible or impossible? Then it reports the Mu'tazila objection: they claim impossibility because allowing karamat would lead to confusion with the mu'jizat (prophetic miracles) of prophets, thereby eliminating any reliable way to distinguish a true prophet from a false one. They argue that breaking natural laws serves the essential purpose of verifying prophethood, which people urgently need, whereas there is no such need to verify a saint. The passage then presents the Sunni (Ahl al-Haqq) position: karamat are not only possible but actually occur, supported by many reports and widespread stories. Two examples are cited: first, the companion of Prophet Solomon who brought the throne of the Queen of Sheba in the blink of an eye—this was not a prophecy of Solomon since it did not occur in conjunction with a claim of prophethood. Second, the story of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab who, while giving a sermon in Medina, saw his army in battle at Nahawand and called out to its commander Sariya to take cover on a mountain; Sariya heard him from a distance of nearly five hundred parasangs, acted on the instruction, and achieved victory. These examples demonstrate that extraordinary events can occur through saints without undermining the distinctiveness of prophetic miracles.

Key Terms
كرامات الأولياء (karamat al-awliya)
معجزة (mu'jiza)
ممكن (mumkin)
ممتنع (mumtani')
نقض العادة (naqd al-'ada)
المتنبي (al-mutanabbi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, why is it impossible for karamat to occur?
A) Because they would be physically impossible.
B) Because they would be confused with prophetic miracles and eliminate the way to know a true prophet.
C) Because no reliable reports exist.
D) Because only prophets can perform miracles.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila deny karamat based on the fear that they would undermine the distinctiveness of prophetic miracles and because there is no necessity for such manifestations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that there is no need to distinguish a saint from a non-saint. Have you ever sought miraculous signs to validate someone's spiritual status, rather than judging by their character and adherence to the Sunna? Will you change that now?
?
If karamat were confused with mu'jizat, your ability to know a true prophet would be lost. On what basis do you truly believe that Muhammad ﷺ is the final prophet? Is your conviction built on the uniqueness of his miracles, or on something deeper, like the message itself?
?
The Mu'tazila insist that breaking natural law only serves prophethood. Then how do you explain the extraordinary events in the lives of awliya that you have heard about? Will you check your own tendency to be impressed by the supernatural, and instead focus on the Qur'an and the prophetic example as the true signs?

The Sunni Affirmation of Karamat with Evidence
Sunni affirmation of karamat with two examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni proof for the occurrence of karamat and the examples used to support it.

Core Idea
Karamat are confirmed by reports and prophetic examples, not denied.

After presenting the Mu'tazila objection, the author turns to the Sunni position (Ahl al-Haqq), who affirm that karamat do occur. They rely on transmitted reports and well-known stories, including events from the time of Solomon and from the early Islamic community. Let's explore the evidence and why these miracles are considered distinct from prophetic miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sunni affirmation
Read the passage where the author states the Sunni position and the type of evidence they use (reports and widespread accounts).
Note that they do not rely on rational arguments but on transmitted reports.
2
Examine the two examples
Look at the two specific examples given: the companion of Solomon and the story of 'Umar. Understand what happened and why they are considered karamat.
Both examples involve extraordinary events that were not performed by a prophet.
3
Consider why these do not cause confusion
Reflect on why these examples do not undermine the distinctiveness of prophetic miracles. How do they differ from mu'jizat?
The key is that no claim of prophethood is involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the companion of Solomon brought the throne of Bilqis in the blink of an eye, does your heart accept that as possible for a non-prophet, or does your mind dismiss it as legend? What does your reaction reveal about how much you truly believe in the limitless power of Allah over His creation? Are you willing to ask Allah to increase your certainty in the unseen realities of karamat?
?
Umar’s voice reached his commander from Medina to Persia, and the commander obeyed. If Allah were to send you a clear sign through a saint today, would you recognize it and respond with immediate obedience, or would you rationalize it away? What is holding you back from a deeper trust in the ways Allah manifests His power through His righteous servants?
?
The people of truth affirmed karamat based on transmitted reports from the past. You have just read these reports, but do you actually live as if karamat are real and possible today? Consider a moment when you desperately needed a miracle—did you call upon Allah through the awliya with hope, or did you rely only on material means? What will you change in your prayers tonight to reflect a deeper belief in the reality of karamat?

The Mu'tazila Argument Against Saintly Miracles
Mu'tazila argument for impossibility of karamat with two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila rational argument for why saintly miracles (karamat) are impossible.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue karamat are impossible because they would confuse with prophetic miracles.

The Mu'tazila, known for their rationalist theology, argued that saintly miracles cannot occur. Their reasoning is based on the need to preserve the distinctiveness of prophetic miracles (mu'jizat). This passage presents their two main arguments, grounded in the purpose of miraculous events.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila state their position clearly: they deem karamat impossible.
The opening phrase 'فزعمت المعتزلة أنه ممتنع' sets the tone.
2
Identify the first argument
The first reason is that if karamat were possible, they would be confused with prophetic miracles (mu'jizat), making it impossible to know who is a true prophet.
This argument hinges on the necessity of distinguishing prophets from non-prophets.
3
Understand the second argument
The second reason is that breaking the natural order (naqd al-'ada) is only beneficial when there is a pressing need, such as identifying a prophet. Since there is no such need for recognizing saints, karamat serve no purpose and thus are impossible.
The Mu'tazila view miracles as purposeful signs, not arbitrary displays.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument reflects the Mu'tazila emphasis on rational coherence and the function of miracles as evidence. How does this differ from the Sunni position that karamat are possible?
This prepares for later lessons on the Sunni response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة الكرامة عند المعتزلة] فزعمت المعتزلة أنه ممتنع (۱) لأنه لو جاز لالتبست المعجزة بالكرامة، ولم يبق إلى الوصول إلى العلم بكون النبي (۲) نبيًا طريق، وهذا ممتنع، ولأن نقض العادة لفائدة الوصول إلى معرفة النبي والتمييز بينه وبين المتنبي وبالناس إلى ذلك حاجة ماسة، فأما الحاجة إلى معرفة الولي من غير الولي فمنعدمة.
The impossibility of karamat according to the Mu'tazila: The Mu'tazila claimed that it is impossible (1) because if it were allowed, the miracle (mu'jiza) would be confused with the saintly miracle (karama), and there would remain no way to attain knowledge that a person is a prophet (2), and this is impossible. And because breaking the customary course of events has a benefit: attaining knowledge of the prophet and distinguishing between him and the false claimant, and people have a pressing need for that. As for the need to know the saint from the non-saint, it is absent.
Source pages p. 781

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila argument for the impossibility of karamat (saintly miracles). It begins by stating their claim that karamat are impossible. They give two reasons. First, if karamat were possible, they would be indistinguishable from mu'jizat (prophetic miracles), and then there would be no way to confirm someone's prophethood. This is unacceptable. Second, breaking the natural order (the customary course of events) is only justified when there is a great benefit: enabling people to recognize a true prophet and distinguish him from false claimants. People urgently need this. In contrast, there is no such pressing need to recognize saints (awliya) from non-saints. Therefore, according to the Mu'tazila, God would not grant such extraordinary events to saints, as they serve no necessary purpose.

Key Terms
كرامة
معجزة
نبي
ولي
معتزلة
نقض العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny karamat because they reject saints or their spiritual status.
Correction: The Mu'tazila base their argument on rational grounds: confusion with prophetic miracles and lack of necessity, not on denial of sainthood itself. They accept the possibility of saints but not extraordinary events for them.

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the Mu'tazila give for the impossibility of karamat?
A) Karamat contradict divine wisdom.
B) Karamat would lead to confusion between prophetic and saintly miracles, blocking knowledge of prophethood.
C) Karamat are not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Saints are not worthy of miracles.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila reject saintly miracles because they threaten the epistemological basis for recognizing true prophets and serve no necessary purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila feared that confusion between prophetic and saintly miracles would undermine the very basis for recognizing a prophet. In your own life, have you ever been so impressed by someone's extraordinary abilities that you gave them authority they did not deserve? How can you safeguard against being misled by outward signs?
?
The passage claims that there is no pressing need to distinguish a saint from a non-saint. Do you agree? Have you ever felt the need to know whether someone is a saint? Or do you prioritize knowing who is a true guide for your religion? If so, how do you determine that?
?
Think about the Mu'tazila's rational approach to this issue. Do you tend to rely more on reason or on transmitted reports when evaluating religious claims? Are you comfortable questioning apparent miracles? How does this lesson challenge your own method of seeking truth?

The Deprivation of Karamat as Proof of Error: The Case of the Mu'tazila
The author argues that Mu'tazila lack of karamat shows error of their beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's lack of karamat, despite their apparent piety, is presented as evidence for the corruption of their beliefs.

Core Idea
Deprivation of karamat despite piety signals doctrinal corruption.

The author now turns to a practical argument against the Mu'tazila: if their beliefs were true, why do they never experience the miracles of saints that are common among Ahl al-Sunna? Their very earnestness in worship, combined with the absence of karamat, points to a deeper spiritual malady. We will explore this argument and the accompanying description of their outward state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument unfold.
Notice the repetition in Arabic; it's a textual variant.
2
Identify the key claim
The author claims that the Mu'tazila deny karamat and are deprived of them due to the ill fortune of their innovation.
3
Examine the conditional argument
The author says even if there were no other evidence, their deprivation of karamat despite devotion suffices to show their error. This is a powerful rhetorical move.
This is an argument from absence.
4
Consider the description of their appearance
The passage ends with a vivid description: a sickly yellowness that repels sight, and burdensome company. What does this add to the argument?
It serves as empirical evidence of their spiritual state.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Karamat are considered signs of God's favor; their absence here indicates divine displeasure. How does this support the Sunni position?
This is a supplementary argument, not a primary one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنكرت المعتزلة ذلك لأنهم حرموا ذلك لشؤم بدعتهم، ولو لم يكن على بطلان مذاهبهم وفساد عقائدهم دليل سوى حرمانهم الكرامة مع جدهم و مع جدهم واجتهادهم في العبادات وشدة توقيهم عن المعاصي والآثام خوفًا من خروجهم عن الإيمان، ثم لم يكن يظهر على أحد منهم كرامة ، ولم يعاين أحد منهم ذلك في نفسه ليرجع عن ه، ويعود إلى الإقرار به، ولم يكن ذلك إلا لبقائهم بسبب عقائدهم الفاسدة أعداء الله تعالى مستحقين للإهانة فيرى الأكثر منهم وقد تعلوهم صفرة سمجة تنبو الأبصار عن النظر إلى طلعته، وتثقل على القلوب معاشرته (۲) وصحبته (۳).
And the Mu'tazila denied that because they were deprived of it due to the ill fortune of their innovation. And if there were no evidence for the falsity of their schools and the corruption of their beliefs except their deprivation of karamat — despite their seriousness, their striving, their devotion in acts of worship, and their intense avoidance of sins and transgressions out of fear of falling from faith — then still no karamat would appear from any of them, and none of them would witness it in himself to turn back from his denial and return to affirming it. And that is only because, due to their corrupt beliefs, they remain enemies of Allah, deserving of humiliation. So you see most of them with a sickly pallor overcoming them, from which eyes recoil in looking at his countenance, and his company and friendship weigh heavily on hearts.
Source pages p. 782

Commentary
This passage offers a supplementary argument against the Mu'tazila position. It states that the Mu'tazila themselves deny karamat, and they are deprived of experiencing them. This deprivation is attributed to the ill fortune (shu'm) of their innovation (bid'a). The author argues that even if there were no other evidence against them, the very fact that despite their apparent earnestness in worship and avoidance of sins — they never exhibit any karamat — is proof of the corruption of their beliefs. They are, due to their corrupt beliefs, enemies of Allah deserving humiliation. The passage ends with a description of their outward state: a sickly yellowness (sufra samija) that repels the sight, and their company is burdensome. This is presented as further evidence of their condition.

Key Terms
بدعة
كرامة
معتزلة
إهانة
صفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that physical appearance or lack of supernatural experiences is a direct measure of faith or piety.
Correction: The passage does not judge individuals by their looks alone; rather, it uses the absence of karamat despite apparent piety as a cumulative argument against a group's doctrines.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the evidence for the falsity of Mu'tazila doctrines besides their denial of karamat?
A) Their lack of scientific knowledge.
B) Their deprivation of karamat despite their apparent devotion.
C) Their rejection of prophetic miracles.
D) Their lack of piety.

Key Takeaway
The deprivation of karamat despite outward piety is a strong sign of doctrinal corruption, according to this author.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says that the Mu'tazila's company is burdensome and their appearance repels. Is there any 'sickly pallor' in your own soul—a spiritual dullness or distance—that you have been ignoring? What would it take for you to honestly examine the beliefs and intentions behind your outward worship today?
?
You strive in worship and avoid major sins, just like the Mu'tazila. But what if your efforts are built on a foundation of incorrect belief about Allah? Would you be willing to question your own assumptions, even if it means admitting error? Are you ready to seek the truth regardless of the cost to your comfort or pride?
?
The Mu'tazila never saw a karama in themselves to turn back. Have you ever witnessed a clear sign from Allah—something that could have changed your direction—but you dismissed it because it didn't fit your expectations? What would it take for you to acknowledge such a sign and let it reshape your faith?

Al-Ash'ari's View on the Servant as Acquirer
Al-Ash'ari's view on servant's acquisition and agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's distinctive position that the servant is called an acquirer and doer but not an agent, and why he reserved the term 'agent' exclusively for Allah.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari distinguishes between acquisition and agency—only Allah is the true agent.

This lesson introduces al-Ash'ari's unique contribution to the debate on human action. While most sects agreed that the servant has an act, al-Ash'ari drew a sharp line: the servant acquires and acts, but cannot be called an agent (fa'il). We examine this distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Ash'ari
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's words come through.
Notice that al-Ash'ari agrees the servant has an act but withholds the title 'agent'.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms muktasib (acquirer), `amil (doer), and fa'il (agent) in the passage. Understand how they are used.
3
Understand why al-Ash'ari denied the servant the title of agent
Reflect on why al-Ash'ari reserved 'agent' for Allah. Think about the theological commitment to divine omnipotence.
4
Reflect on the difference between acquisition and creation
Consider how acquisition differs from creation. The servant acquires an act that God creates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال من سواهم من فرق الأمة بأن العبد مكتسب على الحقيقة وله فعل إلا الأشعري، فإنه زعم أن العبد يسمى مكتسبا عاملا ولا يسمى فاعلا، والفاعل في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى. ثم القائلون بأن العبد له فعل وكسب وعمل اختلفوا من موجد أفعال العباد؟ فزعمت القدرية أن موجدها على الحقيقة هو العبد ولا صنع الله تعالى ولا تصرف في فعل العبد البتة بوجه من الوجوه، غير أن أوائلهم كانوا لا يتجاسرون على إطلاق اسم الخالق على العباد، وكانوا يقولون: إنه موجد فعله ومحدثه ولا يقولون خالقه، وكانوا يساعدون أهل الحق على أن لا خالق إلا الله تعالى إلى أن نشأ بينهم الجبائي فرأى أن لا فرق بين اسم الخالق والموجد فزعم أن كل ما دب ودرج خالق لفعله الاختياري، فلما انتهت نوبة رياستهم إلى أبي عبد الله البصري الملقب بجعل زعم أن الخالق على الحقيقة هو العبد والله تعالى يسمى خالقا على مجاز القول دون الحقيقة. وقال أهل الحق: إن موجدها وخالقها على الحقيقة هو الله تعالى والعبد مكتسب له فاعل له، وساعدهم عليه متكلمو أهل الحديث والنجارية والكرامية، ثم لما كان العبد فاعلا ومكتسبا لابد أن له قدرة واستطاعة إذ لا فعل بدون القدرة، وكذا الكسب عند المقرين به إنما يمتاز عن الأفعال الضرورية بالاستطاعة فنتكلم أولا في القدرة والاستطاعة ثم بعد ذلك نرتقي إلى الكلام في خلق الأفعال إن شاء الله تعالى.
And those besides them from the sects of the Ummah said that the servant is an acquirer (muktasib) in reality and has an act, except al-Ash'ari, for he claimed that the servant is called an acquirer and a doer but is not called an agent, and the agent in reality is Allah the Exalted. Then those who say that the servant has an act, acquisition (kasb), and work differed about who originates the actions of servants? So the Qadariyya claimed that their originator in reality is the servant, and there is no act of Allah the Exalted and no disposal in the act of the servant at all in any way, except that their early ones did not dare to apply the name 'creator' to the servants, and they used to say: He is the originator of his act and its bringer into existence, and they did not say 'his creator', and they used to assist the people of truth that there is no creator but Allah the Exalted, until al-Jubba'i arose among them and saw that there is no difference between the name 'creator' and 'originator', so he claimed that everything that moves and creeps is the creator of its voluntary act. When the turn of their leadership reached Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, nicknamed Ju'ul, he claimed that the creator in reality is the servant, and Allah the Exalted is called creator metaphorically, not in reality. And the people of truth said: Indeed, its originator and creator in reality is Allah the Exalted, and the servant is an acquirer of it and a doer of it; and the theologians of Ahl al-Hadith, al-Najjariyya, and al-Karamiyya assisted them in this. Then, since the servant is an agent and acquirer, he must necessarily have power (qudra) and capability (istita'a), for there is no act without power, and likewise acquisition according to those who affirm it is distinguished from necessary acts only by capability. So we will speak first about power and capability, then after that we will ascend to discourse on the creation of acts, if Allah the Exalted wills.
Source pages p. 785

Commentary
This passage presents a historical and doctrinal survey of the dispute over the creation of human actions. The author begins by noting that most Islamic sects affirm that the servant truly acquires his actions, except al-Ash'ari who denies that the servant is called an agent (fa'il) – only God is the real agent. The Qadariyya claimed the servant is the true originator of his actions, with God having no involvement. Early Qadariyya avoided calling the servant a 'creator', but later figures like al-Jubba'i and Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri explicitly said the servant is the creator. The people of truth (Ahl al-Sunna) hold that God is the true originator and creator, and the servant acquires and does the action. The author then transitions to the necessity of discussing capability (istita'a) because the servant, as agent and acquirer, must have power. The passage concludes by announcing the plan to first discuss power and capability, then the creation of acts.

Key Terms
مكتسب
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ash'ari denied all human involvement in actions.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari affirmed that the servant acquires and does actions; he only denied the title 'agent' to the servant.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, which of the following is the true agent?
A) The servant
B) Allah
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari taught that the servant acquires and does actions, but only Allah is the true agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something, do you genuinely thank Allah as the true agent, or do you secretly credit yourself as the ultimate cause?
?
If only Allah is the true agent, how does that reshape your attitude toward your failures and sins? Do you still feel responsible, or do you rely more on His mercy?
?
In what area of your life do you most struggle to see yourself as an acquirer rather than an agent? What would change if you truly believed that?

The Qadariyya on the Origin of Human Actions
Historical development of Qadariyya on creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evolution of the Qadariyya position from early moderates to later extremists like al-Jubba'i and al-Basri.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya increasingly claimed the servant is the true creator of his own actions.

This lesson traces the Qadariyya's stance on human action. They began by affirming the servant as originator but avoiding the term 'creator'. Over time, figures like al-Jubba'i and al-Basri explicitly asserted that the servant is the creator, challenging the orthodox position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Qadariyya
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three distinct stages presented.
2
Identify the three stages
Find the early Qadariyya, al-Jubba'i, and Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri. Note their differences.
Look for key phrases: 'أوائلهم', 'الجبائي', 'أبو عبد الله البصري'
3
Analyse the shift in terminology
Early Qadariyya said 'originator' but not 'creator'. Al-Jubba'i equated them. Al-Basri said the servant is creator and Allah is creator metaphorically.
4
Compare with the orthodox view
Think about how this contrasts with the Ahl al-Haqq view that Allah is the true creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال من سواهم من فرق الأمة بأن العبد مكتسب على الحقيقة وله فعل إلا الأشعري، فإنه زعم أن العبد يسمى مكتسبا عاملا ولا يسمى فاعلا، والفاعل في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى. ثم القائلون بأن العبد له فعل وكسب وعمل اختلفوا من موجد أفعال العباد؟ فزعمت القدرية أن موجدها على الحقيقة هو العبد ولا صنع الله تعالى ولا تصرف في فعل العبد البتة بوجه من الوجوه، غير أن أوائلهم كانوا لا يتجاسرون على إطلاق اسم الخالق على العباد، وكانوا يقولون: إنه موجد فعله ومحدثه ولا يقولون خالقه، وكانوا يساعدون أهل الحق على أن لا خالق إلا الله تعالى إلى أن نشأ بينهم الجبائي فرأى أن لا فرق بين اسم الخالق والموجد فزعم أن كل ما دب ودرج خالق لفعله الاختياري، فلما انتهت نوبة رياستهم إلى أبي عبد الله البصري الملقب بجعل زعم أن الخالق على الحقيقة هو العبد والله تعالى يسمى خالقا على مجاز القول دون الحقيقة. وقال أهل الحق: إن موجدها وخالقها على الحقيقة هو الله تعالى والعبد مكتسب له فاعل له، وساعدهم عليه متكلمو أهل الحديث والنجارية والكرامية، ثم لما كان العبد فاعلا ومكتسبا لابد أن له قدرة واستطاعة إذ لا فعل بدون القدرة، وكذا الكسب عند المقرين به إنما يمتاز عن الأفعال الضرورية بالاستطاعة فنتكلم أولا في القدرة والاستطاعة ثم بعد ذلك نرتقي إلى الكلام في خلق الأفعال إن شاء الله تعالى.
And those besides them from the sects of the Ummah said that the servant is an acquirer (muktasib) in reality and has an act, except al-Ash'ari, for he claimed that the servant is called an acquirer and a doer but is not called an agent, and the agent in reality is Allah the Exalted. Then those who say that the servant has an act, acquisition (kasb), and work differed about who originates the actions of servants? So the Qadariyya claimed that their originator in reality is the servant, and there is no act of Allah the Exalted and no disposal in the act of the servant at all in any way, except that their early ones did not dare to apply the name 'creator' to the servants, and they used to say: He is the originator of his act and its bringer into existence, and they did not say 'his creator', and they used to assist the people of truth that there is no creator but Allah the Exalted, until al-Jubba'i arose among them and saw that there is no difference between the name 'creator' and 'originator', so he claimed that everything that moves and creeps is the creator of its voluntary act. When the turn of their leadership reached Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, nicknamed Ju'ul, he claimed that the creator in reality is the servant, and Allah the Exalted is called creator metaphorically, not in reality. And the people of truth said: Indeed, its originator and creator in reality is Allah the Exalted, and the servant is an acquirer of it and a doer of it; and the theologians of Ahl al-Hadith, al-Najjariyya, and al-Karamiyya assisted them in this. Then, since the servant is an agent and acquirer, he must necessarily have power (qudra) and capability (istita'a), for there is no act without power, and likewise acquisition according to those who affirm it is distinguished from necessary acts only by capability. So we will speak first about power and capability, then after that we will ascend to discourse on the creation of acts, if Allah the Exalted wills.
Source pages p. 785

Commentary
This passage presents a historical and doctrinal survey of the dispute over the creation of human actions. The author begins by noting that most Islamic sects affirm that the servant truly acquires his actions, except al-Ash'ari who denies that the servant is called an agent (fa'il) – only God is the real agent. The Qadariyya claimed the servant is the true originator of his actions, with God having no involvement. Early Qadariyya avoided calling the servant a 'creator', but later figures like al-Jubba'i and Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri explicitly said the servant is the creator. The people of truth (Ahl al-Sunna) hold that God is the true originator and creator, and the servant acquires and does the action. The author then transitions to the necessity of discussing capability (istita'a) because the servant, as agent and acquirer, must have power. The passage concludes by announcing the plan to first discuss power and capability, then the creation of acts.

Key Terms
قدرية
موجد
خالق
جبائي
أبو عبد الله البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya always held that the servant is the creator.
Correction: Early Qadariyya avoided the term 'creator' and only later figures explicitly claimed it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Jubba'i claim about all moving creatures?
A) They are originators but not creators.
B) They are creators of their voluntary acts.
C) They are mere acquirers.
D) They have no power.

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya progressively claimed that the servant is the true creator of his actions, culminating in the view that Allah is creator only metaphorically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in something, do you genuinely and immediately attribute that success to Allah as the true originator, or do you silently credit your own effort? How does the progression of the Qadariyya from cautious language to outright denial of Allah's creative role mirror subtle shifts in your own heart? What step will you take today to ensure your belief remains that 'لا خالق إلا الله' is not just a phrase but a reality in how you see every action of yours?
?
The Qadariyya ended up saying Allah is 'creator' only metaphorically. In your daily life, are there areas where you treat Allah's role as merely metaphorical—where you speak of reliance on Him but actually trust in your own planning, abilities, or circumstances? What would it look like to truly believe that Allah creates your every action, and what would change in your morning routine if you lived that conviction?
?
The early Qadariyya agreed with Ahl al-Haqq that no creator but Allah, yet they later abandoned that truth. Is there any 'small' compromise in your understanding of tawhid that you think harmless but which could, over time, lead you away from complete reliance on Allah? Name it now, and commit to one specific way you will correct it before this week ends.

The Orthodox Position and the Need for Power
Ahl al-Haqq position on creation and acquisition, and the necessity of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Haqq position that God is the true creator and the servant acquires, and why the discussion of power (qudra) is necessary.

Core Idea
God creates all actions, the servant acquires, and this requires a discussion of human capability.

After surveying deviant views, the author presents the orthodox doctrine: Allah is the real originator and creator, while the servant acquires and does the action. He then notes that since the servant is an agent and acquirer, he must have power and capability, leading into the next topic of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ahl al-Haqq and transition
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two parts: the orthodox statement and the logical transition.
2
Identify the key points of the orthodox view
God is the true originator and creator. The servant is an acquirer and doer. Note the supporting groups.
3
Understand the logical necessity of power
The author argues that because the servant has an act, he must have power (qudra) and capability (istita'a). Ask yourself: why is power necessary for action?
4
See the transition to the next topic
The passage ends by announcing the plan: first discuss power and capability, then the creation of acts. This is a bridge to the next section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال من سواهم من فرق الأمة بأن العبد مكتسب على الحقيقة وله فعل إلا الأشعري، فإنه زعم أن العبد يسمى مكتسبا عاملا ولا يسمى فاعلا، والفاعل في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى. ثم القائلون بأن العبد له فعل وكسب وعمل اختلفوا من موجد أفعال العباد؟ فزعمت القدرية أن موجدها على الحقيقة هو العبد ولا صنع الله تعالى ولا تصرف في فعل العبد البتة بوجه من الوجوه، غير أن أوائلهم كانوا لا يتجاسرون على إطلاق اسم الخالق على العباد، وكانوا يقولون: إنه موجد فعله ومحدثه ولا يقولون خالقه، وكانوا يساعدون أهل الحق على أن لا خالق إلا الله تعالى إلى أن نشأ بينهم الجبائي فرأى أن لا فرق بين اسم الخالق والموجد فزعم أن كل ما دب ودرج خالق لفعله الاختياري، فلما انتهت نوبة رياستهم إلى أبي عبد الله البصري الملقب بجعل زعم أن الخالق على الحقيقة هو العبد والله تعالى يسمى خالقا على مجاز القول دون الحقيقة. وقال أهل الحق: إن موجدها وخالقها على الحقيقة هو الله تعالى والعبد مكتسب له فاعل له، وساعدهم عليه متكلمو أهل الحديث والنجارية والكرامية، ثم لما كان العبد فاعلا ومكتسبا لابد أن له قدرة واستطاعة إذ لا فعل بدون القدرة، وكذا الكسب عند المقرين به إنما يمتاز عن الأفعال الضرورية بالاستطاعة فنتكلم أولا في القدرة والاستطاعة ثم بعد ذلك نرتقي إلى الكلام في خلق الأفعال إن شاء الله تعالى.
And those besides them from the sects of the Ummah said that the servant is an acquirer (muktasib) in reality and has an act, except al-Ash'ari, for he claimed that the servant is called an acquirer and a doer but is not called an agent, and the agent in reality is Allah the Exalted. Then those who say that the servant has an act, acquisition (kasb), and work differed about who originates the actions of servants? So the Qadariyya claimed that their originator in reality is the servant, and there is no act of Allah the Exalted and no disposal in the act of the servant at all in any way, except that their early ones did not dare to apply the name 'creator' to the servants, and they used to say: He is the originator of his act and its bringer into existence, and they did not say 'his creator', and they used to assist the people of truth that there is no creator but Allah the Exalted, until al-Jubba'i arose among them and saw that there is no difference between the name 'creator' and 'originator', so he claimed that everything that moves and creeps is the creator of its voluntary act. When the turn of their leadership reached Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, nicknamed Ju'ul, he claimed that the creator in reality is the servant, and Allah the Exalted is called creator metaphorically, not in reality. And the people of truth said: Indeed, its originator and creator in reality is Allah the Exalted, and the servant is an acquirer of it and a doer of it; and the theologians of Ahl al-Hadith, al-Najjariyya, and al-Karamiyya assisted them in this. Then, since the servant is an agent and acquirer, he must necessarily have power (qudra) and capability (istita'a), for there is no act without power, and likewise acquisition according to those who affirm it is distinguished from necessary acts only by capability. So we will speak first about power and capability, then after that we will ascend to discourse on the creation of acts, if Allah the Exalted wills.
Source pages p. 785

Commentary
This passage presents a historical and doctrinal survey of the dispute over the creation of human actions. The author begins by noting that most Islamic sects affirm that the servant truly acquires his actions, except al-Ash'ari who denies that the servant is called an agent (fa'il) – only God is the real agent. The Qadariyya claimed the servant is the true originator of his actions, with God having no involvement. Early Qadariyya avoided calling the servant a 'creator', but later figures like al-Jubba'i and Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri explicitly said the servant is the creator. The people of truth (Ahl al-Sunna) hold that God is the true originator and creator, and the servant acquires and does the action. The author then transitions to the necessity of discussing capability (istita'a) because the servant, as agent and acquirer, must have power. The passage concludes by announcing the plan to first discuss power and capability, then the creation of acts.

Misconception: Ahl al-Haqq deny human power altogether.
Correction: They affirm human power and capability as necessary for acquisition, but they attribute creation to Allah.

A) The servant
B) Allah

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Haqq affirm that Allah creates all actions, the servant acquires, and this requires a discussion of human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform—every prayer, every word, every sin—is created by Allah, while you are only the acquirer? If so, how does that affect your sense of gratitude for your obedience and your humility after your sins?
?
When you feel you have accomplished something good, do you secretly attribute it to your own power and effort, or do you recognize that even your ability to act is a gift from Allah? How can you train yourself to constantly remember that Allah is the true originator?
?
If your power to act comes from Allah, then your dependence on Him is total. How does this realization change the way you approach your daily responsibilities and your reliance on Him? What specific change can you make today to live out this truth?

The Disagreement on Capability and Its Opposite
Al-Nasafi reports three positions on whether capability applies to both opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major positions on whether capability (istita'a) is suitable for both opposites and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree: capability for an act may or may not be suitable for its opposite—with profound implications for human agency.

In this passage, al-Nasafi reports a crucial debate in kalam about the nature of human capability. The question is whether the same capability that brings about an act is also capable of bringing about its opposite. Three distinct answers emerge, each with significant implications for how we understand human agency and divine power. By examining these positions, we gain insight into the theological differences that shaped early Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups
Read the passage and list the three distinct opinions reported by al-Nasafi. Note who holds each position and the key terminological distinctions (e.g., before the act vs. with the act).
The groups are: those who say capability precedes the act (Mu'tazila), those who say it is with the act (Abu Hanifa, Ash'aris, etc.).
2
Understand the Mu'tazila view
The Mu'tazila argue that capability is like a tool (e.g., a hand) that can be used for either opposite action. They hold this because they consider capability to exist before the act.
3
Explore Abu Hanifa's 'alternation'
Abu Hanifa agrees that capability is suitable for opposites, but only in a sequential sense (badal). The specific instance of capability, once connected to an act, is not simultaneously suitable for the opposite; but if it had been connected differently, it would have been suitable.
This avoids the problem of one capability producing two contradictory acts at once.
4
Analyze the Ash'ari rejection
The Ash'aris and most ahl al-hadith theologians deny that the same capability can be for both opposites. They assign a distinct capability to each act (e.g., capability for faith is different from capability for disbelief).
This position emphasizes divine omnipotence and the specificity of created capabilities.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Consider how each view relates to broader doctrines of free will, divine causality, and moral responsibility. Which view best supports human accountability? Which best safeguards God's omnipotence?
This connects to ongoing debates in kalam about qadar and 'adl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في كونها صالحة للضدين فقال جميع من زعم أن الاستطاعة قبل الفعل : إنها تصلح للضدين كما تصلح لذلك الأسباب والآلات. واختلف القائلون بأنها مع الفعل في ذلك فقال أبو حنيفة: إنها تصلح للضدين على طريق البدل، ومعنى ذلك أن الاستطاعة التي حصل بها الإيمان صلحت له ولا تصلح للكفر إذا اقترنت بالإيمان، ولكنها لو كانت اقترنت بالكفر بدلاً من اقترانها بالإيمان لصلحت له بدلاً من صلاحها للإيمان، وتابعه على هذا القول ابن الراوندي وأبو العباس القلانسي من متكلمي أهل الحديث وأبو العباس ابن سريج من فقهاء أصحاب الحديث. وقالت الأشعرية وجميع متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى القلانسي وابن سريج: إن القدرة لا تصلح للضدين، وإن قدرة الإيمان لا تصلح للكفر وهي غير قدرة الكفر وكذا على القلب، وكذا هذا من قدرة الطاعة وقدرة المعصية وهو قول الحسين بن محمد النجار.
And they disagreed concerning its being suitable for the two opposites. All of those who claimed that capability is before the act said: It is suitable for the two opposites, just as causes and instruments are suitable for that. And those who said that it is with the act disagreed concerning that. Abu Hanifa said: It is suitable for the two opposites by way of alternation, and the meaning of that is that the capability by which faith occurs is suitable for it and is not suitable for disbelief if it is conjoined with faith, but if it had been conjoined with disbelief instead of being conjoined with faith, it would have been suitable for it instead of being suitable for faith. He was followed in this opinion by Ibn al-Rawandi, Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi among the theologians of ahl al-hadith, and Abu al-Abbas Ibn Surayj among the jurists of ahl al-hadith. The Ash'ariyya and all the theologians of ahl al-hadith except al-Qalanisi and Ibn Surayj said: Capability is not suitable for the two opposites, and the capability of faith is not suitable for disbelief, and it is other than the capability of disbelief, and similarly conversely, and similarly this applies to the capability of obedience and the capability of disobedience. This is the opinion of al-Husayn ibn Muhammad al-Najjar.
Source pages p. 791

Commentary
This passage presents a key disagreement in kalam about whether the capability (istita'a) for an action is also suitable for its opposite. Al-Nasafi first notes that all those who hold that capability precedes the act (mainly the Mu'tazila) affirm that it is suitable for both opposites, just as physical instruments (e.g., a hand) can be used for either action. Then he turns to those who hold that capability is simultaneous with the act, and reports Abu Hanifa's view: capability is suitable for opposites 'by way of alternation' (badal). That means a particular instance of capability, once it is actually connected to a specific act, is not simultaneously suitable for its opposite; but if it had been connected to the opposite instead, it would have been suitable for that opposite. This avoids the problem of one capability producing two contradictory acts at once. Al-Nasafi lists supporters: Ibn al-Rawandi, al-Qalanisi, and Ibn Surayj. Then he presents the opposing view of the Ash'aris and most theologians of ahl al-hadith: capability is specific to its act and not suitable for its opposite. They deny that the same capability can be for both faith and disbelief; each act has its own distinct capability. Al-Nasafi ends by attributing this position also to al-Husayn al-Najjar.

Key Terms
استطاعة
ضد
بدل
قدرة
إيمان
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Abu Hanifa's 'alternation' means that the same capability simultaneously produces both opposites.
Correction: Abu Hanifa means that the capability is suitable for the opposite only hypothetically—if it had been attached to the opposite instead. It does not actually produce both at once.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa's view as reported, what does it mean for capability to be suitable for opposites 'by way of alternation'?
A) The same capability can produce both faith and disbelief at the same time.
B) The capability that brings about faith would have been equally capable of bringing about disbelief if it had been conjoined with it instead.
C) Capability is not suitable for any opposite act.
D) The capability for faith is different from the capability for disbelief.

Key Takeaway
The suitability of capability for opposites is a key point of contention that distinguishes major schools in kalam, with implications for human agency and divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose between obedience and disobedience, do you truly believe that the same capability you have for one could have been used for the other? Or do you feel that at the moment of sin, you 'had no choice'? How does that feeling align with the positions in this lesson?
?
Abu Hanifa said that if the capability had been conjoined with disbelief, it would have been suitable for it— but in reality, you committed faith or sin. What does that say about your responsibility for the actual deed? Are you willing to own that the capability God gave you was specifically used for that act, and you could have used it differently?
?
The Ash'aris argue that capability for faith is distinct from capability for disbelief. If that is true, then your salvation is entirely dependent on which capability God creates in you. Does that humble you or trouble you? How should this affect your reliance on God and your gratitude for the capability He did give you?

Mu'tazila Proofs for the Precedence of Capability
Mu'tazila arguments for capability preceding action using Qur'an and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qur'anic and rational arguments used by the Mu'tazila to prove that capability (istita'a) must precede the act.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila used Qur'anic commands and rational necessity to argue that capability must be present before action.

After noting al-Maturidi's neutral presentation of the debate, al-Nasafi introduces the critical question: does capability precede the act or is it simultaneous? He then presents the Mu'tazila's scriptural and rational evidence for the precedence of capability. This lesson examines those proofs, focusing on how they interpret two key verses and a logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that al-Nasafi first describes al-Maturidi's approach before presenting the Mu'tazila proofs.
The passage is cut off at the end of the rational argument.
2
Identify each proof
The passage contains three distinct proofs: two from the Quran and one rational argument. Find each one and note the verse or reasoning.
3
Reflect on the rational argument's conclusion
The rational argument is incomplete, but the intended conclusion is clear: if capability did not precede faith, God's command would be impossible. Consider how this connects to the concept of taklif ma la yutaq.
Think about why the Mu'tazila considered this a strong argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي ذكر الخلاف وذكر الحجج لكل فريق ولم يشتغل بالجواب لحجج أحد الفريقين، ولم يظهر أنه إلى أي قول يميل، وتكلم عن المعتزلة على الطريقين جميعًا، وأكثر كلامه يدل أنه يميل إلى أنها لا تصلح للضدين. فنتكلم في أن الاستطاعة متقدمة على الفعل أم مقارنة له، ويدخل الكلام في أكثر الفصول تحت الكلام في هذه المسألة. فاحتجت المعتزلة بقوله تعالى: فَاتَّقُوا اللَّهَ مَا اسْتَطَعْتُمْ فينبغي أن يكون كل من لزمه التقوى مع عدم التقوى إذ غير المتقي لزمه التقوى، فينبغي أن يكون معه استطاعة التقوى وفي وجود استطاعة التقوى ولا تقوى، قول متقدم استطاعة التقوى على التقوى. واحتجوا أيضا بقوله: خُذُوا مَا ءَاتَيْنَكُم بِقُوَّةٍ والأخذ بقوة لن يتصور أن تكون القوة سابقة على الأخذ كالأخذ باليد لن يتصور إلا وأن تكون اليد سابقة عليه. والمعقول لهم أن القدرة لو لم تكن سابقة على الفعل ولم تكن مع الكافر قدرة الإيمان سابقة على الإيمان لكان الأمر له بالإيمان محالاً لما فيه من تكليف ما لا
And the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned the disagreement, and mentioned the proofs for each group, and did not occupy himself with answering the proofs of either group, and it did not appear to which opinion he inclines; but he spoke about the Mu'tazila on both ways, and most of his speech indicates that he inclines to the view that it is not suitable for the two opposites. So we shall speak about whether capability precedes the act or is concomitant with it, and the discussion of many sections falls under the discussion of this issue. The Mu'tazila argued by His saying, exalted: 'So fear Allah as much as you are able'; therefore everyone upon whom piety is obligatory must have, while lacking piety (since the non-pious person has piety obligatory upon him), must have with him the capability for piety; and in the existence of the capability for piety without piety, (this) indicates the precedence of the capability for piety over piety. They also argued by His saying: 'Take what We have given you with strength'; and the taking with strength cannot be conceived that the strength precedes the taking, like taking with the hand cannot be conceived except that the hand precedes it. And their rational argument is that if capability were not antecedent to the act, and there were not with the disbeliever a capability for faith antecedent to faith, then the command to him to have faith would be impossible because it entails the imposition of what is not...
Source pages p. 792

Commentary
This passage first reports al-Maturidi's handling of the earlier debate on capability and opposites. Al-Nasafi notes that al-Maturidi presented the arguments from both sides without giving a definitive answer, but his overall treatment suggests he tends to the view that capability is not suitable for opposites. Then al-Nasafi announces he will now discuss whether capability precedes the act or is simultaneous, a question that underlies many other discussions. He then presents the proofs of the Mu'tazila for the precedence of capability. First, they cite the verse 'So fear Allah as much as you are able' (Q. 64:16). They reason: the command to fear Allah applies to all, including those who are not currently pious. Since they are commanded to fear Allah, they must have the capability to do so. The existence of that capability in them while they lack the actual piety shows that the capability must be present before the act of piety. Second, they cite the verse 'Take what We have given you with strength' (Q. 7:171). They argue that the 'taking with strength' necessarily implies that the strength (capability) is prior to the taking, just as the hand must be prior to the act of taking. Finally, they present a rational argument: if capability were not prior to the act, and a disbeliever had no capability for faith prior to his actual faith, then God's command to him to have faith would be impossible, because it would be commanding what he cannot do (taklif ma la yutaq). The argument is cut off, but the intended conclusion is clear.

Key Terms
استطاعة
تقوى
قوة
تقدم
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila are simply proving that capability exists.
Correction: They are specifically arguing for its temporal precedence before the act, not merely its existence.

Checkpoint
Which of the following verses did the Mu'tazila use to argue that capability must precede the act?
A) 'And Allah is the Creator of all things' (Q. 13:16)
B) 'So fear Allah as much as you are able' (Q. 64:16)
C) 'No burden do We place on any soul but that which it can bear' (Q. 23:62)
D) 'And We have certainly made the Quran easy for remembrance' (Q. 54:17)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila relied on scriptural commands and logical necessity to establish that capability must be present before the commanded act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are commanded to do something difficult in your faith, do you assume you already have the capability to do it? Or do you make excuses that you lack the strength? The Mu'tazila argued that the very command implies the capability. How does this challenge your tendency to shrink from difficult acts of obedience? What is one specific command you have been avoiding, believing you lack the ability, and what will you do about it starting today?
?
If capability precedes action, then what is your excuse for not acting on the truths you already know? You have the capability to fear Allah—so why don't you? Reflect on a moment today when you knew the right thing to do but did not do it. Was it really a lack of ability, or a lack of will? What will you change to ensure your next action matches your capability?
?
The Mu'tazila used rational arguments to prove that God's commands are never impossible. Do you live as if you believe that? Or do you treat some commands as beyond you? What would change if you truly internalized that God never commands you to do something without giving you the ability to do it? Consider a command from the Qur'an or Sunnah that you have dismissed as 'too hard'. How will you approach it now, knowing the capability is there?

Subsistence Requires a Substrate
Al-Nasafi states the impossibility of subsistence without a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why subsistence, as an accident, cannot exist without a substrate according to Sunni kalam.

Core Idea
Subsistence is an accident; accidents cannot exist without a substrate.

The Karramiyya sect claimed that subsistence could exist independently of any substrate. Imam al-Nasafi refutes this by establishing the foundational principle that all temporal attributes like subsistence are accidents and thus require a substrate. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Nasafi uses a very direct style here.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The passage begins by quoting the Karramiyya claim that subsistence can exist without a substrate. Identify that statement.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Nasafi argues that subsistence is temporal and an accident, and accidents require a substrate. See how he uses general principle (all accidents need a substrate) to refute the specific claim.
This is a foundational proof in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبقى من أجل بقاء لا في محل، وفي تقرر استحالة وجود بقاء غير قائم بمحل ما يبطل كلامه، لأن البقاء الحادث عرض، والعرض لا يوجد بدون محل كما في سائر أنواع الأعراض، وقد بينا قبل هذا ما يوجب بطلان ذلك ويقال له: إذا كان البقاء موجودًا لا في محل فما الذي جعل ذاتاً متعينًا أولى بأن يكون باقيًا به من ذات آخر سواه؟ ولم كان هذا بقاء له دون غيره؟ ويقال له: إنك أطلقت القول باستحالة بقاء الحركات والإرادات فما الذي يمنع الله تعالى من أن يقول لهما: ابقيا ؟ ويطالب بالفرق بين الحركات والإرادات، وبين غيرهما من الأعراض ولن يجد سبيلا إليه ويقال للكرامية : إذا قال الله تعالى لذات "ابق". يبقى ذلك الذات ببقاء أم بلا بقاء؟ فإن قالوا : بلا بقاء، أحالوا ، وإن قالوا يبقى ببقاء، قلنا: فالبقاء معنى زائد على الذات، أم راجع إلى الذات؟ فإن قالوا: معنى زائد على الذات فقد انقادوا للحق وظهر بطلان مذهبهم في القول بجواز اتصاف الأعراض بالبقاء لاستحالة قيام الأعراض بالأعراض وإن قالوا: البقاء راجع إلى الذات والباقي باق لذاته لا لمعنى وهو مذهبهم، نبطل عليهم هذا الكلام بعد هذا - إن شاء الله تعالى.
He remains from the sake of subsistence not in a substrate, and in the determination of the impossibility of the existence of subsistence not subsisting in a substrate there is what invalidates his statement, because temporal subsistence is an accident (ʿaraḍ), and an accident does not exist without a substrate as in all other kinds of accidents. And we have explained before this what necessitates the nullity of that. And it is said to him: if subsistence exists not in a substrate, then what made a particular essence more deserving to be subsistent by it than another essence other than it? And why was this subsistence for it rather than another? And it is said to him: you have spoken absolutely about the impossibility of the subsistence of motions and wills – so what prevents God most high from saying to them: 'subsist!'? And he is asked for the difference between motions and wills and other accidents, and he will not find a way to it. And it is said to the Karramiyya: when God most high says to an essence 'subsist!', does that essence subsist by a subsistence or without subsistence? If they say: without subsistence, they commit an impossibility. And if they say: it subsists by subsistence, we say: is subsistence an additional meaning (maʿnan zāʾid) to the essence, or referring back to the essence? If they say: an additional meaning to the essence, then they have yielded to the truth and the nullity of their doctrine in saying the permissibility of accidents being qualified by subsistence has become apparent, because of the impossibility of accidents subsisting in accidents. And if they say: subsistence refers back to the essence and the subsistent is subsistent by itself (li-dhātihi) not by a meaning – and that is their doctrine – we will refute this statement against them after this, if God wills.
Source pages p. 796

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Karramiyya sect regarding the attribute of subsistence (al-baqāʾ). The author begins by arguing against an opponent (likely a Karrami) who claims that subsistence can exist without a substrate (lā fī maḥall). The author states that temporal subsistence (al-baqāʾ al-ḥādith) is an accident (ʿaraḍ), and accidents do not exist without a substrate, as is the case with all types of accidents. He then presents an objection: if subsistence exists without a substrate, then what makes one specific essence more deserving to be subsistent by it than another? There is no reason for it to be assigned to one rather than another. Next, the author poses a dilemma to the Karramiyya: if God commands motions and wills to 'subsist', what prevents Him from doing so? The opponent claims subsistence of motions and wills is impossible, but the author demands a distinction between these and other accidents, which the opponent cannot provide. Finally, the author directly addresses the Karramiyya: when God says to an essence 'subsist!', does that essence subsist with a subsistence or without it? If without, it is impossible (since everything that exists requires an attribute to persist). If with a subsistence, then the question is whether that subsistence is an additional meaning (maʿnan zāʾid) over the essence or merely refers back to it. If they say it is additional, they concede the truth and their own doctrine falls because they allow accidents (like subsistence) to qualify other accidents, which is impossible. If they say it refers back to the essence and the essence is subsistent by itself without any added meaning, the author promises to refute this later. Thus, the Karramiyya position is shown to be contradictory.

Key Terms
بقاء (subsistence)
عرض (accident)
محل (substrate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Subsistence can exist without a substrate like an independent entity.
Correction: Subsistence is an accident, and all accidents require a substrate to exist. Therefore, subsistence cannot exist independently.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why must subsistence have a substrate?
A) Because it is eternal
B) Because it is an accident
C) Because it is a divine attribute
D) Because it is a substance

Key Takeaway
Subsistence, being an accident, cannot exist without a substrate; thus the Karramiyya claim is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that everything other than God is completely dependent on Him for its continued existence? How does this truth affect the way you rely on people, possessions, or your own abilities? What would it mean to live today as if only God is self-subsistent?
?
The Karramiyya claimed that subsistence could exist without a substrate—a subtle denial of God's sole self-subsistence. Are there areas in your life where you unconsciously treat something as independent of God, as if it could 'subsist' on its own? Will you identify one such area and actively remind yourself of your utter need for Allah?
?
Al-Nasafi argues that even temporal subsistence is an accident requiring a substrate. Everything created is dependent. When you consider your own existence, do you feel that dependency in your heart, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge your need for Allah's constant preservation?
