The Argument from Specificity
Al-Nasafi poses a rhetorical question about specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if subsistence existed without a substrate, there would be no reason for it to be assigned to one essence over another.

Core Idea
Without a substrate, subsistence has no basis for being specific to one essence.

Al-Nasafi continues his refutation by posing a logical challenge: if subsistence could exist without a substrate, what would determine which essence it attaches to? This argument highlights the absurdity of the Karramiyya position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic sentence and its translation. This is a single rhetorical question.
2
Understand the logical problem
If subsistence is independent of any substrate, then there is no reason for it to be associated with one essence rather than another. This is a classic argument against ungrounded attributes.
This is similar to arguments used in proving the oneness of God.
3
Appreciate the force of the question
The question 'Why this subsistence for this essence rather than another?' cannot be answered by the Karramiyya without invoking a substrate or a cause, which they deny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What is the main point of this argument?
A) Subsistence must have a substrate or else its assignment is arbitrary.
B) Subsistence can exist without a substrate.
C) Essences are all equal in deserving subsistence.
D) God's command is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
If subsistence exists without a substrate, its assignment to any particular essence would be arbitrary, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are specific to Him without any substrate, what logical basis do you have for that specificity other than blind acceptance? How does that sit with your pursuit of truth?
?
What would it mean for your faith if a core attribute of God were arbitrarily assigned? Would you still trust His wisdom and justice?
?
When you hold beliefs about God's nature, do you ever check if they are logically consistent—or do you rely on comfort? Will you examine one of your deeply held beliefs today using the same rigor Al-Nasafi applies?

The Argument from Motions and Wills
Al-Nasafi challenges the inconsistency regarding motions and wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Karramiyya cannot consistently deny subsistence to motions and wills while allowing it to other accidents.

Core Idea
If God can command motions and wills to subsist, why can't they?

Al-Nasafi challenges the Karramiyya's arbitrary distinction: they claim subsistence of motions and wills is impossible, yet they allow subsistence for other accidents. He demands a justification for this difference, which they cannot provide.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the objection.
2
Identify the inconsistency
The opponent says motions and wills cannot subsist. Al-Nasafi asks why God could not command them to subsist. This exposes a double standard.
3
Understand the demand for distinction
Al-Nasafi challenges the opponent to find a difference between motions/wills and other accidents. The opponent cannot.
This shows the opponent's position is arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What does al-Nasafi demand from the opponent in this argument?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, when questioned, turned out to be based on no real distinction? When you realize that, will you have the courage to admit it, or will you cling to it like the opponent in this passage?
?
If God's power extends to commanding even your most stubborn inclinations to persist or cease, what does that mean for the ways you've been resisting change in your life? Are you ready to let God command your 'motions' and 'wills'?
?
When someone challenges your assumptions and demands a clear distinction, do you feel threatened or curious? What would it cost you to be as rigorous with your own beliefs as Al-Nasafi is with the Karramiyya?

The Dilemma of God's Command
Al-Nasafi presents a dilemma on God's command to subsist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma that traps the Karramiyya when God commands an essence to subsist.

Core Idea
When God says 'subsist', the essence either subsists with or without subsistence – both lead to problems.

In this final argument, al-Nasafi presents a powerful dilemma to the Karramiyya. If God commands an essence to subsist, the essence must either subsist with a subsistence or without. Both options lead to contradictions that undermine their entire position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This is a detailed dilemma with multiple branches.
2
Trace the dilemma
Follow the two main branches: with or without subsistence. Then within 'with subsistence', there are two sub-branches: subsistence as additional meaning or referring back to essence.
Use the guided breakdown below.
3
Understand the outcome
Both options either lead to admitting subsistence is an added attribute (which refutes their claim that accidents can have subsistence) or to a position they hold but will be refuted later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He then presents an objection: if subsistence exists without a substrate, then what makes one specific essence more deserving to be subsistent by it than another? There is no reason for it to be assigned to one rather than another. Next, the author poses a dilemma to the Karramiyya: if God commands motions and wills to 'subsist', what prevents Him from doing so? The opponent claims subsistence of motions and wills is impossible, but the author demands a distinction between these and other accidents, which the opponent cannot provide. Finally, the author directly addresses the Karramiyya: when God says to an essence 'subsist!', does that essence subsist with a subsistence or without it? If without, it is impossible (since everything that exists requires an attribute to persist). If with a subsistence, then the question is whether that subsistence is an additional meaning (maʿnan zāʾid) over the essence or merely refers back to it. If they say it is additional, they concede the truth and their own doctrine falls because they allow accidents (like subsistence) to qualify other accidents, which is impossible. If they say it refers back to the essence and the essence is subsistent by itself without any added meaning, the author promises to refute this later. Thus, the Karramiyya position is shown to be contradictory.

Key Terms
بقاء (subsistence)
ذات (essence)
معنى زائد (additional meaning)
كرامية (Karramiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what happens if the Karramiyya say that subsistence is an additional meaning?
A) They admit the truth and their doctrine is nullified
B) They are correct
C) They must provide another proof
D) They avoid the dilemma

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya are caught in a dilemma: either subsistence is an added attribute (contradicting their denial of accidents qualifying accidents) or the essence is self-subsistent (a view to be refuted).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's attributes (like His eternity) are not separate from His essence, even though your mind struggles to grasp this? How does this belief affect the way you trust in His unchanging nature?
?
When you affirm that God is eternal, do you sometimes think of eternity as an added 'quality' that He possesses? This passage shows the danger of such a view. Will you commit to examining your own theological assumptions, even if they are comfortable, to ensure they are free from inconsistency?
?
The Karramiyya held a view that led to a logical contradiction. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that, upon honest reflection, might contain hidden inconsistencies? What will you do to resolve them?

Contradiction in the Mu'tazila's Distinction of Attributes
Nasafi exposes a contradiction in the Mu'tazila's own criterion for attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the Mu'tazila's own criterion for attributes to expose an internal contradiction in their theology.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own rule for attributes forces them to accept real attributes in creation, contradicting their denial of God's attributes.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi takes a sharp polemical turn. He quotes the Mu'tazila's own definition of two types of attributes — attributes of the self (necessary) and attributes of meaning (separable) — and then shows that by that definition, even an atom must have a real attribute (permanence) that comes and goes. This exposes a contradiction: if created things have real attributes, why deny them for God? The passage is a masterful application of internal critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Aim to capture the overall flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage begins with 'And what verifies this is...' – it is part of a larger discussion.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila criterion
Find the exact definition the Mu'tazila use to distinguish between an attribute of the self and an attribute of meaning. Write it down in your own words.
Attribute of the self: what is necessary for the thing. Attribute of meaning: what can depart from it.
3
See the application to God
The Mu'tazila apply this criterion to God: knowledge and power are necessary, so they are attributes of the self, not superadded. Understand their conclusion.
They use this to deny that God has real, distinct attributes.
4
Understand the counterexample
Nasafi then considers the atom (jawhar). Examine how permanence (baqa') behaves: it can depart from the atom and, in fact, must depart initially. Determine what this implies by the Mu'tazila's own rule.
Permanence is an attribute of meaning for the atom, meaning it is a real, superadded quality.
5
Grasp the contradiction
Reflect on why this is a contradiction for the Mu'tazila. If they admit that permanence is a real attribute for atoms, they cannot consistently deny real attributes for God without a special justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق هذا أن عبارة القدرية في إثبات التفرقة بين صفة النفس وصفة المعنى أن صفة النفس ما لزمه وصفة المعنى ما جاز خروجه عنه، وعلى هذا بنوا قولهم بنفي الصفات عن القديم عز ذكره، وقالوا: لم يجز خروجه عن الوصف بالعلم والقدرة ولزم الوصف بهما، فكانا صفتي النفس دون المعنى، وقد رأينا الجوهر جاز خروجه عن الوصف بالبقاء في أول أحوال وجوده بل وجب فتبين أنه صفة معنى على أصلهم أيضا، وهذه مناقضة لهم ظاهرة.
And what verifies this is that the statement of the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) in establishing the distinction between attribute of the self (sifat al-nafs) and attribute of meaning (sifat al-ma'na) is that the attribute of the self is what is necessary for it, and the attribute of meaning is what it is possible for it to depart from. And upon this they built their statement negating attributes from the Eternal (Exalted is His mention). They said: 'It is not possible for Him to depart from the description of knowledge and power, and the description of them is necessary, so they are attributes of the self, not of meaning.' And we have seen that the atom can depart from the description of permanence in the first state of its existence—rather, it is necessary [to depart]—so it becomes clear that it is an attribute of meaning according to their own principle. And this is an apparent contradiction for them.
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage is an argument that the Mu'tazila's own criterion for distinguishing attributes leads to the conclusion that permanence is an attribute of meaning, contradicting their denial of real attributes. The author explains the Mu'tazila principle: an attribute of the self (صفة النفس) is one that cannot be separated from the thing (e.g., knowledge and power for God, they claim), while an attribute of meaning (صفة المعنى) is one that can be absent. The Mu'tazila use this to negate real attributes for God, arguing that God's knowledge and power are necessary and thus not superadded. However, the author points out that in the case of the atom, permanence is not only possible to depart from (as the atom exists without it initially) but actually necessary to depart from (since at first it is impermanent). Therefore, by the Mu'tazila's own rule, permanence for the atom is an attribute of meaning, i.e., a superadded reality. This exposes a self-contradiction: if they accept permanence as a real attribute for atoms, they cannot consistently deny similar attributes for God without special justification.

Key Terms
صفة النفس (sifat al-nafs: attribute of the self)
صفة المعنى (sifat al-ma'na: attribute of meaning)
القدرية (al-Qadariyya: the Mu'tazila)
القديم (al-Qadim: the Eternal, God)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Nasafi is endorsing the Mu'tazila's distinction between attributes.
Correction: Nasafi is using their own distinction to expose an internal contradiction, not agreeing with it.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila distinction cited, which of the following is an attribute of meaning?
A) God's power, because it is necessary.
B) The atom's permanence, because it can depart.
C) God's knowledge, because it cannot depart.
D) The atom's existence, because it is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's own criterion for attributes forces them to admit real attributes in creation, contradicting their denial of real attributes for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assess your own beliefs, do you apply the same standard of consistency to yourself that you demand of others? Have you ever clung to a principle that, if honestly applied, would force you to admit something you are unwilling to accept?
?
The Mu'tazila built a rational system that seemed logical until tested against a simple example. Are there areas of your faith where your own reasoning might be leading you to conclusions that contradict what Allah has revealed? Are you willing to let revelation correct your reason, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
This lesson exposes an intellectual contradiction. Contemplate a time when you were shown an inconsistency in your own thinking about deen. Did you admit it, or did you resist? What would it take for you to surrender your intellectual pride for the sake of truth?

Refuting the Karramiyya Claim on Divine Annihilation
Nasafi refutes the Karramiyya claim that God's 'Fan' command annihilates persistent accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya objection to the argument for accident persistence and why Nasafi dismisses it as invalid.

Core Idea
Divine command cannot override logical impossibility.

The Karramiyya sect proposed that even if an accident persists by itself, God could directly command it to annihilate, causing its non-existence. Nasafi rejects this as corrupt, but his argument is cut off. In this lesson, we examine the objection and Nasafi's initial response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation; note that the passage ends mid-sentence due to a page break.
The argument is incomplete; we only have the beginning.
2
Identify the objection and refutation
The Karramiyya claim: God's command 'Fan' can annihilate a persistent accident. Nasafi calls it false and begins explaining why, but the text breaks off.
Notice the use of the term 'bāṭil' (false) and 'fāsid' (corrupt).

This passage is a brief refutation of a specific objection from the Karramiyya sect. The author dismisses this as invalid (bāṭil) without completing the argument, presumably because the page ends. The reasoning that is partially given is that according to the Karramiyya, God's command causes non-existence in the second moment, but this is corrupt because the persistent accident would still exist with the command? The text breaks off, so only the initial critique is available.

Misconception: Divine omnipotence can override any logical impossibility.
Correction: In classical kalam, even God cannot bring about logical contradictions, such as a thing being both existent and non-existent at the same time.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya claim that God can annihilate a persistent accident by command is invalid because it would imply a logical impossibility.

Challenge to al-Jubba'i and His Son on Accident Persistence
Author challenges al-Jubba'i and his son on the persistence of fleeting accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that if accidents persist by themselves, then even fleeting accidents like sounds and movements should persist, and the principle that origination conditions persistence.

Core Idea
The persistence of accidents is impossible if their origination is conditioned.

This lesson presents a key challenge from the author to the views of al-Jubba'i and his son. They claim accidents persist by themselves, but the author questions why certain accidents do not persist and formulates a principle linking origination to persistence. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the Maturidi stance on accident persistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the question addressed to al-Jubba'i and his son, and the formulation that follows.
The passage lists several accidents: sounds, pains, movements, etc.
2
Identify the principle
The author says: 'That in which the origination of something is not sound... is not sound for a different description upon it after existence.' Understand that this means the description at origination determines persistence.
This principle is key to the argument.
3
Apply the principle to the examples
The author applies the principle to the accidents that opponents agree cannot persist. If they could persist, their description would change from non-persistent at origination to persistent after, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للجبائي وابنه إذا كان الباقي باقيًا لذاته، فما الذي منع من القول بجواز بقاء الصوات والآلام والحركات والفكر والإرادات والكراهات والفناء؟ وكذا يقال لأبي هاشم: ما الذي منع من القول بجواز بقاء الكلام؟ ويحرر هذا، ويقول: ما لا يصح حدوث شيء فيه أوله أو به لم يصح بغير الوصف عليه بعد الوجود، كهذه الأعراض التي وافقتمونا على استحالة القول ببقائها، ولو قلنا ببقاء الأعراض لتغير الوصف عليها لما بينا أنها في حالة الحدوث ليس بموصوفة بالبقاء. ولا معنى لقول البهشمية إن الكلام إنما لا ينفي للحاجة إلى قطعه واتباعه بجنسه على صورة النظم والتأليف، لأن ما يتبعه إذا لم يكن ضدا له حاصلاً في محله لا يوجب المنع من بقائه؛ ولأن العلة المانعة عن بقائه لو كان هذا لكان يجوز بقاء الحرف الواحد ذلك لا يجوز، دل أن المانع ما ذكرنا من استحالة قيام ومع البقاء به فيشاركه جميع ما يستحيل ذلك عليه، ولأن الحكميات هي التي تعتبر الحاجة وعدم الحاجة لا الحقائق بل يجب النظر فيها إلى ما يوجب التغير أو إلى الوجود والعدم، فإذا هذا التعليل منه جهل مفرط.
And it is said to al-Jubba'i and his son: If the persistent [accident] is persistent by itself, then what prevents the permissibility of the persistence of sounds, pains, movements, thoughts, wills, dislikes, and annihilation? Likewise it is said to Abu Hashim: What prevents the permissibility of the persistence of speech? And this is formulated [as follows]: He says: That in which or by which the origination of something is not sound in its first [moment] is not sound for a different description upon it after existence, like these accidents with which you agree with us on the impossibility of asserting their persistence. And if we said that accidents persist, the description upon them would change, because they are not described as persistent in the state of origination. And there is no meaning to the Bahshamiyya's statement that speech is not negated [i.e., does not persist] due to the need to interrupt it and follow it by its kind in the form of composition and arrangement, because what follows it, if it is not an opposite occurring in its subject, does not necessitate prevention of its persistence. And because if the preventing cause for its persistence were this, then the persistence of a single letter would be permissible, and that is not permissible. This indicates that the preventer is what we mentioned: the impossibility of subsistence together with persistence, so that all [accidents] for which that is impossible share [the same rule]. And because prescriptive matters (hukmiyyat) are those which consider need and lack of need, not real natures; rather, one must consider in them what necessitates change or existence and non-existence. So this explanation of theirs is extreme ignorance.
Source pages p. 805

Commentary
This passage is a two-part argument against the opponents. First, the author addresses al-Jubba'i and his son (the Jubba'iyya) and Abu Hashim (the Bahshamiyya) by asking: if accidents persist by themselves, why are certain accidents like sounds, pains, movements, etc. not said to persist? The author then formulates a principle: anything that cannot originate in a certain state or by a certain means also cannot persist after existence with a different description. For example, these accidents that even the opponents agree cannot persist—if we were to say they persist, their description would change, because in the moment of origination they are not described as persistent. Then the author refutes the Bahshamiyya's specific explanation for why speech does not persist: they claim it is because speech needs to be interrupted and followed by its kind (like letters in a sequence). The author counters that what follows speech is not an opposite in its subject, so it does not prevent persistence. Furthermore, if that were the cause, then a single letter could persist, but it does not. So the real cause is the impossibility of subsistence with persistence, which applies to all such accidents. The author also distinguishes between prescriptive matters (hukmiyyat), which depend on need, and real natures (haqa'iq), which do not; the opponents' error is confusing these, revealing their ignorance.

Key Terms
جبائي (al-Jubba'i)
باقي (persistent)
صوت (sound)
ألم (pain)
حركة (movement)
فكر (thought)
إرادة (will)
كراهة (dislike)
فناء (annihilation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author merely lists accidents without logical reasoning, but he actually provides a principle that links origination to persistence.
Correction: The author's principle shows that the impossibility of persistence is derived from the nature of origination, not arbitrary.

Checkpoint
What is the author's key principle in this passage?
A) Accidents persist by themselves.
B) That which cannot originate as persistent cannot later become persistent.
C) All accidents are non-persistent.
D) Speech persists.

Key Takeaway
The persistence of accidents is impossible because their origination conditions their description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief, do you follow its logical implications even when they lead to uncomfortable conclusions? Or do you stop short to protect your comfort? What belief are you afraid to examine fully today?
?
The author demands consistency: if you claim a principle, you must apply it to all cases. In your life, where are you inconsistent in applying the truths you claim to believe? Will you identify one area and commit to aligning your actions with your convictions this week?
?
This lesson shows that even in theological disputes, intellectual honesty requires following reason to its end. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on tradition or feeling rather than rigorous reasoning? What would it cost you to seek clarity, and are you willing to pay that price for truth?

7 min
Refutation of the Bahshamiyya on the Persistence of Speech
Author refutes Bahshamiyya's explanation for why speech does not persist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of the Bahshamiyya's explanation for why speech does not persist, and why the real reason is the impossibility of subsistence with persistence.

Core Idea
The Bahshamiyya's claim that speech must be interrupted is not the real cause; it is the impossibility of subsistence with persistence.

This lesson focuses on the author's specific refutation of the Bahshamiyya's view on speech. The Bahshamiyya argued that speech does not persist because it needs to be interrupted and followed by other speech. The author counters that this does not logically prevent persistence, and points to the case of a single letter to show the fallacy. He concludes that the true preventing cause is the impossibility of subsistence together with persistence, which applies to all such accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the Bahshamiyya's argument
First, understand the Bahshamiyya's position: speech does not persist because it must be cut off and followed by its kind (letters) in composition.
They think this interruption is necessary for speech to be understood.
2
Follow the author's first counterargument
The author argues that what follows speech is not an opposite in the same subject, so it does not prevent persistence. If it were an opposite, it might, but it is not.
This shows the Bahshamiyya's cause is not necessary.
3
Analyze the second counterargument with the single letter
If the preventing cause were the need to interrupt, then a single letter could persist because it doesn't need interruption. But a single letter also does not persist, so the cause is different.
This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the real cause and the distinction
The real preventer is the impossibility of subsistence together with persistence. The author also distinguishes between prescriptive matters and real natures, criticizing the Bahshamiyya's confusion.
This shows the opponents' ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author agrees that speech must be interrupted to persist, but he refutes that.
Correction: The author shows that the need for interruption does not logically prevent persistence, and the true cause is different.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to refute the Bahshamiyya's claim that speech must be interrupted to persist?
A) The example of a single letter that does not persist.
B) The claim that speech is eternal.
C) The argument that speech has no opposite.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
The real reason speech does not persist is the impossibility of subsistence with persistence, not the need for interruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Bahshamiyya confused prescriptive matters with real natures. In your own life, are there areas where you treat humanly constructed rules as if they are divine realities? What is one such area, and how will you re-examine it today?
?
The author calls the Bahshamiyya's explanation 'extreme ignorance.' How can you be sure that your understanding of Allah's attributes, like His speech, is not similarly based on flawed assumptions? Write down one belief about Allah that you hold without deep reflection, and commit to verifying it against revelation and sound reason.
?
This lesson shows that a wrong cause leads to logical contradictions. In your worship, what is an action you do for a reason that you have never critically examined? Will you investigate its true basis today?

The Mu'tazila Inconsistency in Self-Identification
Rhetorical critique of Mu'tazila self-identification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi critique of the Mu'tazila's self-identification as 'people of justice' and 'choice' given their denial of human capacity.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own doctrine contradicts their proudest titles.

Imam al-Nasafi here delivers a sharp rhetorical critique of the Mu'tazila, highlighting the contradiction between their self-ascribed labels—'people of justice' and 'people of choice'—and their actual theological positions. He argues that they deny human capacity for action while claiming to defend free will and justice. This lesson unpacks that critique and its implications for understanding early kalam polemics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the full force of the author's rhetorical tone.
2
Identify the contradictions
Note each claim the Mu'tazila make about themselves and the counter-evidence the author provides.
There are three specific contradictions.
3
Understand the labels
Grasp what 'jabr', 'ikhtiyar', 'adl', and 'tajwir' mean in this context.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does the author call it 'ultimate impudence or ignorance'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العجب من وقاحة المعتزلة وقلة حيائهم حيث ينسبون إلى الجبر من يقول: إن المكلف يفعل عن قدرة لا عن عجز، ويسمون أنفسهم القائلين بالاختيار مع زعمهم أن لا قدرة للفاعل على فعله ويسمون أنفسهم أهل العدل مع زعمهم أن الله تعالى يعذب العباد على أفعال لا قدرة لهم عليها، ويكلفهم بأفعال لا تعلق لها باستطاعة المكلف بوجه من الوجوه، ويسمون القائلين بأنه تعالى لا يعذب أحدًا على فعل لا قدرة له عليه ولا يأمره بذلك ولا ينهاه عنه أهل التجوير. وهذا غاية الوقاحة أو نهاية الجهل والغباوة.
Then amazement at the impudence of the Mu'tazila and their lack of modesty, for they attribute to jabr (compulsion) the one who says that the obligated person acts through capacity not incapacity, and they call themselves the proponents of choice despite their claim that the agent has no capacity for his action, and they call themselves the people of justice despite their claim that God punishes servants for actions over which they have no capacity, and imposes on them obligations that have no connection whatsoever to the servant's ability, and they call those who say that He does not punish anyone for an action over which he has no capacity, nor command or prohibit him thereby, the people of injustice (tajwīr). And this is the ultimate impudence or the extreme of ignorance and stupidity.
Source pages p. 812

Commentary
This passage is a rhetorical critique of the Mu'tazila. The author points out the inconsistency in their self-identification. They accuse others of jabr (compulsion) while themselves denying that the agent has capacity for his action. They call themselves 'people of choice' (ahl al-ikhtiyār) while holding that the agent has no power over his act. They call themselves 'people of justice' (ahl al-'adl) while claiming that God punishes people for acts they cannot perform and imposes obligations they cannot fulfill. They label those who affirm that God does not punish without capacity as 'people of injustice' (ahl al-tajwīr). The author concludes that this is sheer impudence or ignorance.

المعتزلة
الجبر
الاختيار
العدل
التجوير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazila are being accused of jabr (compulsion) themselves.
Correction: Actually, the author accuses them of inconsistency: they call themselves proponents of choice and justice while holding positions that undermine human capacity and divine justice.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do the Mu'tazila call themselves despite denying capacity for the act?
A) People of justice and choice.
B) People of compulsion and injustice.
C) People of knowledge and wisdom.
D) People of faith and piety.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's self-identification as 'people of justice and choice' contradicts their denial of human capacity and their assertion that God punishes for impossible acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Some Sunni Scholars Call the Mu'tazila 'Mujbira'
Rhetorical reversal of labels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some Sunni scholars inverted the label 'Jabriyya' and applied it to the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila are actually the true compellers.

This brief passage from Imam al-Nasafi highlights a remarkable rhetorical reversal. While the Mu'tazila accused the Jabriyya of denying human agency, some Sunni verifiers turned the accusation around, calling the Mu'tazila themselves 'mujbira' (compellers). Let's explore why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the key terms: 'muḥaqqiqīn', 'Mu'tazila', and 'mujbira'.
The phrase 'kathīran mā kāna yaqūl' indicates repeated usage.
2
Understand the inversion
Think about the doctrinal context: the Mu'tazila deny that humans have any real capacity. By denying capacity, they effectively make God the sole agent, which is the position of the Jabriyya. Thus, the label 'mujbira' fits them.
The preceding discussion (not shown) explains the reasoning behind this inversion.
3
Reflect on the doctrinal point
Consider how labels can be rhetorically reversed. Why might this be an effective argument? What does it reveal about the Sunni view of Mu'tazili theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا الحرف كان بعض المحققين من أهل السنة يسمى المعتزلة مجبرة وكثيرا ما كان يقول : قالت المعتزلة المجبرة كذا
And for this reason, some of the verifiers (muḥaqqiqīn) from Ahl al-Sunna used to call the Mu'tazila 'mujbira' (compellers), and often he would say: 'The Mu'tazila, the compellers, said such and such.'
Source pages p. 812

Commentary
This is a brief historical or methodological note. The author states that some Sunni scholars (muḥaqqiqīn) would refer to the Mu'tazila as 'mujbira' (compellers), reversing the usual accusation. Since the Mu'tazila deny that the agent has any genuine capacity, they are the ones who effectively compel the agent, thus deserving the label 'mujbira' more than those they accuse.

Key Terms
المجبرة
المعتزلة
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila are being called 'mujbira' in the same sense as the Jabriyya.
Correction: It is a rhetorical inversion: the Mu'tazila are called 'mujbira' because they deny human capacity, effectively compelling the agent. The label is applied polemically, not as a doctrinal equivalence.

Checkpoint
What does the author say some Sunni verifiers called the Mu'tazila?
A) People of justice.
B) Compellers (mujbira).
C) Disbelievers.
D) Innovators.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's denial of human capacity led some Sunni scholars to call them 'mujbira' (compellers), turning the accusation back on them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I had no choice,' are you unknowingly aligning yourself with the Mu'tazila's error of denying human agency? How can you reclaim your accountability today?
?
The Mu'tazila were accused of being 'compellers' because their logic eliminated human responsibility. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are compelled, and how can you take ownership instead?

The Mu'tazila Objection: Action Without Power or Incapacity
Imam al-Nasafi reports the Mu'tazila argument that the agent is neither capable nor incapable at the moment of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that at the moment of action, the agent is neither capable nor incapable, and why this challenges the Maturidi position on power and action.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claim the agent at the moment of action is neither powerful nor incapable.

Imam al-Nasafi reports an objection from the Mu'tazila who argue that if an act can occur without power in the second moment, then the agent cannot be said to be powerful (since power is absent) nor incapable (since the act occurs). This intermediate state challenges the standard view that human action depends on power. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim of an intermediate state
Read the passage and note that the Mu'tazila assert the state of existence of the act is between the state of power and the state of incapacity.
This is the starting point of their objection.
2
Follow the argument from absence of power
The act occurs in the second moment without power. The Mu'tazila argue the agent does not become incapable (because the act occurs) nor remains powerful (because power is absent). Identify this reasoning.
3
Examine why the agent is not powerful
The Mu'tazila argue that a powerful agent has the ability to leave the act, but leaving an existing act is impossible, so at the moment of action the agent is not powerful. Find this point in the text.
This is a key part of their argument.
4
Examine why the agent is not incapable
An incapable person cannot have the act occur from him, but here the act does occur, so the agent is not incapable. Confirm this in the text.
5
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this argument challenges the Maturidi position that human acts require power. Why might the Mu'tazila be raising this objection?
The common misunderstanding is that they deny agency entirely – they are instead highlighting a logical difficulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام للمعتزلة حالة وجود الفعل حالة وسط بين حالة القدرة وحالة العجز ثم إن الكلام في جانب الفعل - وهو أن حصوله لما جاز مع عدم القدرة في الثاني جاز مع عدمها في العاشر - حمل بعض المعتزلة على أن زعم أن القدرة وإن انعدمت في الثاني فالفاعل لن يصير في تلك الحال موصوفًا بالعجز ولن يبقى موصوفًا بالقدرة، فالفعل يحصل ممن ليس بقادر ولا عاجز، ولن يتصور وجود الفعل إلا ممن هذا وصفه، وزعم أن القادر على الفعل قادر على تركه، وترك الموجود مستحيل فلم يكن هو حال وجود الفعل قادرًا لأنه ليس بقادر على تركه، لأن تركه مستحيل فلا يدخل تحت القدرة، وزعم أن العاجز غیر موجود منه ما هو عاجز عنه ، وهذا وجد منه الفعل فلم يكن قادرًا ولا عاجزاً.
A statement of the Mu'tazila: the state of existence of the act is an intermediate state between the state of power (qudra) and the state of incapacity ('ajz). Then, the discourse concerning the side of the act—that its occurrence, because it is permissible with absence of power in the second moment, is permissible with its absence in the tenth moment due to their equality in non-existence—led some of the Mu'tazila to claim that even if power is absent in the second moment, the agent will not become described as incapable in that state nor remain described as powerful. So the act occurs from someone who is neither powerful nor incapable, and it is inconceivable for the act to exist except from one with this description. And they claimed that the one who has power over an act has power to leave it, but leaving an existing act is impossible, so he is not powerful at the moment of the act's existence because he is not able to leave it; since leaving it is impossible, it does not fall under power. And they claimed that the incapable person does not have what he is incapable of occurring from him, but here the act occurs from him, so he is neither powerful nor incapable.
Source pages p. 819

Commentary
This passage is a report of the Mu'tazila's objection. It begins by stating their claim: the state of existence of the act is an intermediate state between having power (qudra) and being incapable ('ajz). The argument is based on the earlier premise that the act can occur without power in the second moment. The Mu'tazila reason that when power is absent, the agent is not described as incapable (because the act occurs) nor as powerful (because power is absent). Therefore, the act proceeds from someone who is neither powerful nor incapable. They further argue that a powerful agent has the ability to leave the act, but leaving an existing act is impossible, so at the moment of action the agent is not powerful. Conversely, an incapable person cannot have the act occur from him, but here the act does occur, so he is not incapable. Thus, the agent at the moment of action is neither powerful nor incapable. This is a critique of the Maturidi position, aiming to show a logical problem in the relationship between power and action.

Key Terms
حالة القدرة
حالة العجز
القادر
العاجز
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila deny human agency entirely.
Correction: They are instead pointing out a logical difficulty in the standard conception of power and action.

Checkpoint
What do the Mu'tazila conclude about the agent at the moment of action, according to this passage?
A) He is powerful.
B) He is incapable.
C) He is neither powerful nor incapable.
D) He is both powerful and incapable.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that at the moment of action, the agent is neither powerful nor incapable, posing a challenge to the Maturidi concept of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila claim that at the very moment you act, you are not powerful over your action. Does this unsettle your feeling of control? How would your daily choices change if you truly believed you had no power at the instant of doing?
?
If the state of action is 'between power and incapacity', where do you sense yourself when you sin? Do you excuse yourself as 'not having control' or do you feel the weight of responsibility? How does this lesson challenge that self-perception?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being told that at every good deed you performed, you were neither powerful nor incapable. Would that make you more humble or more anxious about your accountability? What would you say to Him about your role in those actions?

The Mu'tazilite Claim and Three Disputed Questions
Nasafi enumerates the Mu'tazilite claim and three disputed questions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim of an intermediate state between power and incapacity and the three key questions that frame the dispute.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites posit an intermediate where none is conceivable.

Imam Nasafi begins by identifying the Mu'tazilite position that there exists a middle state between power and incapacity. This stems from their view that action cannot occur when either power or incapacity is present. The passage then presents three central questions that define the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation to understand the Mu'tazilite claim about an intermediate state.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بين أمرين لا يعقل بينهما واسطة'.
2
Identify the three questions
Notice how the passage lists three disputes: whether action with power is impossible, whether power must be present during action, and whether an intermediate exists.
These questions are the core of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه أثبت الواسطة بين أمرين لا يعقل بينهما واسطة بناء على ما ينتحله من المذهب من استحالة الفعل عند وجود القدرة أو العجز، فكان وجوده دليلاً على انتفاء ما يستحيل ثبوته عنده وهو القدرة والعجز جميعًا، والنزاع في هذا أن ثبوت الفعل مع القدرة عليه هل هو محال أم لا؟ وأن وجود القدرة حال وجود الفعل هل هو واجب أم لا؟ وأن القدرة والعجز هل بينهما واسطة أم لا؟ فمن أراد أن يجعل الفعل الذي هو دليل على مقارنة القدرة عليه إياه عند خصومه دليلاً على انتفاء القدرة فقد قلب الدليل، وجعل دليل الثبوت دليل العدم من غير دليل سوى إرادته تصحيح مقالته، وكذا إذا جعل ذلك دليل ثبوت الواسطة فقد قلب الدليل إذ هو دليل ثبوت القدرة لا دليل زوالها، ولهذا كان الفعل دليل قدرة الفاعل لا دليل زوالها. ثم نقول لهم : أيستحيل ثبوت الفعل من العاجز لوجود العجز أم لانعدام القدرة ؟ فإن قالوا لوجود العجز قيل لهم: ينبغي أن لا يكون الفعل عن الجمادات والموات مستحيلاً لانعدام العجز لها وينبغي أن لا يكون الفعل دليل كون الفاعل قادرًا بل يكون دليل أنه ليس بعاجز خصوصاً على قولكم : إذا كانت بينهما عندكم واسطة، وذلك كله فاسد.
Then he affirmed the intermediate between two things between which no intermediate is conceivable, based on his adopted doctrine of the impossibility of action when power or incapacity exists. So its existence became evidence for the negation of what he deems impossible to establish, namely power and incapacity together. The dispute about this is: is the existence of action together with power impossible or not? And is the existence of power at the moment of action obligatory or not? And is there or is there not an intermediate between power and incapacity? So whoever wants to make the action, which is evidence of the concomitance of power to it according to his opponents, into evidence of the negation of power has reversed the evidence and made evidence of existence into evidence of non-existence without any proof except his desire to validate his statement. Likewise, if he makes that evidence for the existence of the intermediate, he has reversed the evidence, for it is evidence of the existence of power, not evidence of its removal. For this reason, action is evidence of the power of the agent, not evidence of its removal. Then we say to them: Is the existence of action from the incapable impossible due to the existence of incapacity or due to the absence of power? If they say: due to the existence of incapacity, they are told: it should be that action from inanimate objects and dead things is not impossible, because they lack incapacity. And it should be that action is not evidence of the agent being powerful but evidence that he is not incapable, especially according to your view when there is an intermediate between them in your opinion. All of that is corrupt.
Source pages p. 823

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that there is a middle state between power (qudra) and incapacity ('ajz). Nasafi begins by stating that the opponent posits an intermediate between two things that logically cannot have an intermediate. The opponent bases this on his view that action (fi'l) is impossible when either power or incapacity exists, so the existence of action indicates the negation of both power and incapacity simultaneously. Nasafi points out that the dispute is about three questions: whether action with power is impossible, whether power must exist at the moment of action, and whether there is an intermediate. He then argues that if someone uses the action (which is evidence of the coexistence of power) as evidence for the negation of power, they have reversed the evidence. Similarly, using it to prove the intermediate is also a reversal, because action is evidence of power, not its removal. Nasafi then poses a question to the opponent: Is the impossibility of action from the incapable due to the presence of incapacity or the absence of power? If due to incapacity, then action should be possible from inanimate objects (which lack incapacity), and action would only indicate the absence of incapacity, not the presence of power—which is absurd. Therefore, the opponent's position is invalid.

Key Terms
الواسطة
القدرة
العجز
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That there is a middle state between power and incapacity because action occurs only when neither is present.
Correction: Action is evidence of power, not its absence or a middle state.

Checkpoint
What does the Mu'tazilite claim about the relationship between action and power/incapacity?
A) Action is impossible only when power is present.
B) Action is impossible when either power or incapacity is present.
C) Action is possible only when both power and incapacity are absent.
D) Action is always possible regardless of power or incapacity.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim of an intermediate state is based on a flawed premise that action cannot coexist with power or incapacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own obedience to God, do you ever assume a middle state between having the power to obey and being incapable? How does this lesson warn you against such rationalization, and what would it mean to accept that every action you take reflects either your capability or your helplessness before Allah?
?
In what areas of your life are you tempted to claim 'I couldn't help it' instead of admitting you had the power but chose otherwise? How does the rejection of an intermediate between ability and inability call you to greater personal accountability?
?
Reflect on a moment when you attributed your failure to circumstances rather than your own choice. If there is no third state, how must you reinterpret that event in light of your actual power at the time?

The Reversal of Evidence and the Conclusive Argument
Nasafi critiques the reversal of evidence and delivers a final decisive argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's critique that using action as evidence for the absence of power or for an intermediate is a reversal of proof, and his conclusive argument about the nature of incapacity.

Core Idea
Action is evidence of power, not its removal.

In this passage, Nasafi delivers the core logical refutation. He argues that the Mu'tazilites have reversed the evidentiary value of action: instead of proving power, they claim it proves the absence of power. Then he poses a decisive question that exposes the absurdity of their position.

Guided Path
1
Examine the reversal argument
Read how Nasafi argues that using action (evidence of power) to prove its negation is a reversal of proof.
Notice the phrase 'قلب الدليل'.
2
Follow the final argument
Read the question about whether action is impossible due to incapacity or absence of power, and see the absurd consequence.
This argument shows the flaw in the Mu'tazilite view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه أثبت الواسطة بين أمرين لا يعقل بينهما واسطة بناء على ما ينتحله من المذهب من استحالة الفعل عند وجود القدرة أو العجز، فكان وجوده دليلاً على انتفاء ما يستحيل ثبوته عنده وهو القدرة والعجز جميعًا، والنزاع في هذا أن ثبوت الفعل مع القدرة عليه هل هو محال أم لا؟ وأن وجود القدرة حال وجود الفعل هل هو واجب أم لا؟ وأن القدرة والعجز هل بينهما واسطة أم لا؟ فمن أراد أن يجعل الفعل الذي هو دليل على مقارنة القدرة عليه إياه عند خصومه دليلاً على انتفاء القدرة فقد قلب الدليل، وجعل دليل الثبوت دليل العدم من غير دليل سوى إرادته تصحيح مقالته، وكذا إذا جعل ذلك دليل ثبوت الواسطة فقد قلب الدليل إذ هو دليل ثبوت القدرة لا دليل زوالها، ولهذا كان الفعل دليل قدرة الفاعل لا دليل زوالها. ثم نقول لهم : أيستحيل ثبوت الفعل من العاجز لوجود العجز أم لانعدام القدرة ؟ فإن قالوا لوجود العجز قيل لهم: ينبغي أن لا يكون الفعل عن الجمادات والموات مستحيلاً لانعدام العجز لها وينبغي أن لا يكون الفعل دليل كون الفاعل قادرًا بل يكون دليل أنه ليس بعاجز خصوصاً على قولكم : إذا كانت بينهما عندكم واسطة، وذلك كله فاسد.
Then he affirmed the intermediate between two things between which no intermediate is conceivable, based on his adopted doctrine of the impossibility of action when power or incapacity exists. So its existence became evidence for the negation of what he deems impossible to establish, namely power and incapacity together. The dispute about this is: is the existence of action together with power impossible or not? And is the existence of power at the moment of action obligatory or not? And is there or is there not an intermediate between power and incapacity? So whoever wants to make the action, which is evidence of the concomitance of power to it according to his opponents, into evidence of the negation of power has reversed the evidence and made evidence of existence into evidence of non-existence without any proof except his desire to validate his statement. Likewise, if he makes that evidence for the existence of the intermediate, he has reversed the evidence, for it is evidence of the existence of power, not evidence of its removal. For this reason, action is evidence of the power of the agent, not evidence of its removal. Then we say to them: Is the existence of action from the incapable impossible due to the existence of incapacity or due to the absence of power? If they say: due to the existence of incapacity, they are told: it should be that action from inanimate objects and dead things is not impossible, because they lack incapacity. And it should be that action is not evidence of the agent being powerful but evidence that he is not incapable, especially according to your view when there is an intermediate between them in your opinion. All of that is corrupt.
Source pages p. 823

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that there is a middle state between power (qudra) and incapacity ('ajz). Nasafi begins by stating that the opponent posits an intermediate between two things that logically cannot have an intermediate. The opponent bases this on his view that action (fi'l) is impossible when either power or incapacity exists, so the existence of action indicates the negation of both power and incapacity simultaneously. Nasafi points out that the dispute is about three questions: whether action with power is impossible, whether power must exist at the moment of action, and whether there is an intermediate. He then argues that if someone uses the action (which is evidence of the coexistence of power) as evidence for the negation of power, they have reversed the evidence. Similarly, using it to prove the intermediate is also a reversal, because action is evidence of power, not its removal. Nasafi then poses a question to the opponent: Is the impossibility of action from the incapable due to the presence of incapacity or the absence of power? If due to incapacity, then action should be possible from inanimate objects (which lack incapacity), and action would only indicate the absence of incapacity, not the presence of power—which is absurd. Therefore, the opponent's position is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the existence of action means the agent lacks both power and incapacity.
Correction: Action indicates the presence of power, not its absence.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say about using action as evidence for the intermediate state?
A) It is a valid proof.
B) It is a reversal of evidence.
C) It is inconclusive.
D) It proves the intermediate.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that action is evidence of the agent's power, and any attempt to use it to prove otherwise involves a logical reversal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own actions throughout the day, do you truly see them as evidence of Allah's power at work in you, or do you unconsciously claim them as your own independent achievements? What would change if you began every action by acknowledging that it is only possible because Allah grants you power in that moment?
?
Nasafi argues that using action to prove the absence of power is a reversal of evidence. In your own life, do you ever take the blessings Allah gives you—like health, wealth, or ability—and use them to argue that you have no need of Him? How does this passage expose the corruption of that attitude?
?
If action is evidence of power, then every time you sin, your action still testifies to Allah's power—but what does it say about your use of that power? Does this lesson make you feel a deeper accountability for how you employ the capacity Allah gives you?
